General Principles of Toxicology
Unit I
death is that the number of mitochondria undergoing MPT (which 
probably depends on the degree of chemical exposure) determines 
the fate of the cell. According to this model, when only a few mito-
chondria develop MPT, they are removed by selective autophagy 
(mitophagy; see the section “Cellular Repair”) and the cell survives. 
When more mitochondria suffer MPT, the autophagic mechanism 
becomes overwhelmed, and proapoptotic factors are released to 
initiate caspase activation, and when MPT involves virtually all 
mitochondria, necrosis occurs (Orrenius et al., 2011). Based on the 
mechanistic understanding of necroptosis and ferroptosis, cell death 
mediated by these pathways involves triggering mechanisms that 
differ from necrosis or apoptosis, but they still converge on altered 
mitochondrial function.
Induction of Cell Death by Unknown Mechanisms  Some 
toxicants cause cell death by mechanisms other than necrosis or 
programmed cell death pathways. For example, chemicals that 
directly damage the plasma membrane, such as lipid solvents, 
detergents, and venom-derived hydrolytic enzymes will cause 
direct cell rupture (discussed earlier). Additionally, xenobiotics 
that damage the lysosomal membrane lead to tissue degradation by 
release of lysosomal hydrolases, as is the case for aminoglycoside 
antibiotics and hydrocarbons binding to α2u-globulin that injure 
renal tubular cells. Other mechanisms of cell death include destruc-
tion of the cytoskeleton, such as that observed with microfilament 
toxins (e.g., phalloidin and cytochalasins) and microtubule toxins 
(e.g., colchicine and 2,5-hexanedione), the protein phosphatase 
inhibitor, microcystin, and toxicants that disrupt protein synthesis 
(e.g., α-amanitin and ricin).
Impairment of External Cellular Maintenance  Toxicants may 
also interfere with cells that are specialized to provide support to 
other cells, tissues, or the whole organism. Chemicals acting on 
the liver illustrate this type of toxicity. Hepatocytes produce and 
release into the circulation a number of proteins and nutrients. They 
remove cholesterol and bilirubin from the circulation, converting 
them into bile acids and bilirubin glucuronides, respectively, for 
subsequent excretion into bile. Interruption of these processes may 
be harmful to the organism, the liver, or both. For example, inhi-
bition of hepatic synthesis of coagulation factors by dicoumarols 
(warfarin) does not harm the liver, but may cause death by hemor-
rhage. Although used clinically in anticoagulant therapy, this same 
mechanism is responsible for its rodenticidal action. In the fast-
ing state, inhibitors of hepatic gluconeogenesis, such as hypogly-
cin (the ackee fruit–derived causative agent of Jamaican vomiting 
sickness; methylene cyclopropyl alanine), may be lethal by limit-
ing the supply of glucose to the brain. Similarly, Reye syndrome, 
considered to result from hepatic mitochondrial injury caused 
by a combination of a viral disease (which may induce hepatic 
NOS) and intake of salicylate (Pugliese et al., 2008), causes not 
only hepatocellular injury but also severe metabolic disturbances 
(hypoglycemia and hyperammonemia) that affect other organs. 
Chemical interference with the β-oxidation of fatty acids or the 
synthesis, assembly, and secretion of lipoproteins overloads the 
hepatocytes with lipids, causing steatosis and hepatic dysfunction 
(Pessayre et al., 2012).
STEP 4: INAPPROPRIATE REPAIR  
AND ADAPTATION
The final steps in the development of toxicity are inappropriate 
repair and adaptation (Fig. 3-1). Progression of toxic lesions can be 
intercepted by repair mechanisms operating at molecular, cellular, 
and tissue levels as illustrated in Fig. 3-23. Cells and organisms 
also adapt to toxicity, which renders them more resistant to antici-
pated adverse effects.
Mechanisms of Repair
Molecular Repair  Damaged molecules may be repaired in 
numerous ways. Some chemical alterations, such as oxidation 
of protein thiols and methylation of DNA, are simply reversed. 
Hydrolytic removal of the damaged unit and insertion of a newly 
synthesized, correct unit often occur with chemically altered DNA 
or protein and peroxidized lipids. In some instances, the damaged 
molecule is totally degraded and resynthesized. This process is 
time-consuming but unavoidable in cases such as the regeneration 
of cholinesterase after organophosphate intoxication.
Repair of Proteins: Thiol Groups  As discussed earlier, thiol 
groups are essential for the function of many proteins. Oxidation 
of protein thiols, protein disulfides, protein–glutathione mixed 
disulfides, and protein sulfenic acids is reversed by reduction (Ray 
et al., 2012) (Fig. 3-24), and thioredoxins and glutaredoxins, small 
proteins that feature active cysteine residues, are the major endog-
enous protein-reducing enzymes. There are two isoenzymes of 
these proteins and thioredoxin reductase that are distinguished by 
intracellular location (cytosolic and mitochondrial, respectively) 
(Sies et al., 2017). When the catalytic thiol groups in these proteins 
are oxidized, they are reduced by NADPH generated by isocitrate 
dehydrogenase, glucose-6-phosphate dehydrogenase, and 6-phos-
phogluconate dehydrogenase. ROS may also oxidize methionines 
in proteins into sulfoxides (protein-Met-S=O), forming both the 
S and R epimers, which can be reduced by methionine sulfoxide 
reductase (Msr) A and B enzymes, respectively (Moskovitz, 2005). 
Msr enzymes reduce oxidized methionines with concurrent oxida-
tion of their catalytic cysteine to sulfenic acid (Msr-Cys-S-OH), and 
the modified Msr reacts with a neighboring thiol to form an intramo-
lecular disulfide or with glutathione to form a protein–glutathione 
disulfide. The activity of the oxidized enzyme is restored by reduc-
tion catalyzed by thioredoxin or glutaredoxin, with subsequent 
steps depicted in Fig. 3-24. Reduction of methionine sulfoxides 
in lens proteins (e.g., α-crystallin) is especially critical for main-
tenance of the transparency of the eye lens, and it has been shown 
that MsrA knockout mice develop cataract on repeated exposure 
to hyperbaric oxygen (Kantorow et al., 2012). Repair of oxidized 
hemoglobin (metHb) occurs by means of electron transfer from

Repair of Proteins: Heat Shock Chaperones  Soluble intracel-
lular proteins such as cytosolic enzymes are typically folded into a 
globular form with hydrophobic amino acid residues folded inter-
nally, hydrophilic residues located externally, and a ligand (sub-
strate) binding site that resides in a hydrophobic cleft. Physical or 
chemical insults may evoke an unduly large opening of this cleft, 
leading to protein denaturation and aggregation. Molecular chaper-
ones such as the heat-shock proteins (Hsp; e.g., Hsp90, Hsp70, and 
Hsp40) can prevent protein unfolding by “clamping down” onto the 
exposed hydrophobic region of their client protein, using energy of 
ATP hydrolysis to execute a conformational change that is required 
for this maneuver. Hsp90 is critical for stabilizing the cleft and 
impeding further unfolding, which would eventually lead to deg-
radation of the protein by the ubiquitin–proteasome system (UPS) 
described below (Schopf et al., 2017). This important function can 
be blocked by compounds such as geldanamycin and herbimycin 
that promote degradation of Hsp90-bound proteins by the UPS.
The process of ubiquitination and the interaction with the heat-
shock protein response is illustrated in Fig. 3-25. Hsp 90 and Hsp70 
have opposing effects on protein stability. Hsp90 stabilizes proteins 
and prevents ubiquitination, whereas Hsp70 promotes ubiquitination 
and proteosomal degradation. Ubiquitin (Ub) is a small (8.6 kDa) 
protein that directs target proteins to proteosomal degradation when 
covalently linked to the target. Ubiquitination is a posttranslational 
protein modification that takes place in an enzymatic cascade com-
posed of three types of proteins—the E1 Ub-activating enzyme, the 
E2 Ub-conjugating enzyme, and the E3 Ub ligase. E1 first activates 
Ub by adenylating its terminal carboxyl group to form Ub-AMP, 
and then it transfers Ub from Ub-AMP to its catalytic SH group, 
forming a high-energy thioester bond between E1 and Ub. In the 
subsequent transthiolation reaction, Ub is transferred from E1 to 
the catalytic SH group of E2. Thereafter, E3 binds both the target 
protein and the Ub-charged E2 and catalyzes the transfer of Ub 
from E2 to the target protein, forming an isopeptide bond between 
the terminal carboxyl group of Ub and an ε-amino group of a lysine 
residue in the substrate. Multiple rounds of this process gener-
ate polyubiquitin chains. In general, mono-ubiquitination triggers 
endocytosis or autophagy of the protein, whereas substrates that are 
tagged with Lys48-linked Ub chains are recognized and degraded 
by the 26S proteasome into small peptides. In contrast, proteins 
with Lys63-linked Ub chains are essential components of signal-
ing pathways, functioning as scaffolds to assemble signaling com-
plexes, as is the case for NF-κB.
In the case of Hsp functions, dissociation of Hsp90 from the 
unfolding protein allows Hsp70 and its co-chaperone Hsp40 to 
recruit the E3 Ub ligases (e.g., CHIP and Parkin) to tag the protein 
with a polyubiquitin chain (Bedford et al., 2011) so that the protein 
is degraded in proteasomes. The 26S proteasome is large barrel-
shaped multiprotein complex that binds the ubiquitinated protein, 
deubiquitinates and unfolds it using ATP, and finally hydrolyzes 
the protein with its threonine-containing protease active sites into 
small peptides (Fig. 3-25). The UPS controls the cellular level of 
numerous regulatory proteins (e.g., p53, IκB, Nrf2, β-catenin, and 
cyclins) and has a prominent role in eliminating oxidized or oth-
erwise damaged and unfolded intracellular proteins (Kriegenburg 
et al., 2011). For example, the reductive dechlorination of CCl4

by CYP2E1 causes the heme in the cytochrome to become cross-
linked to the trichloromethyl free radical it forms, and this inactive 
enzyme is eliminated by proteasomal degradation. Oligomerization 
and aggregation of damaged and unfolded proteins preclude the 
proteasome from degrading them. Such substrates can even trap 
proteasomes, rendering them nonfunctional. After ubiquitination, 
protein aggregates can also be eliminated by autophagy, a process 
described in more detail in the section “Cellular Repair.”
Repair of Lipids  Phospholipids containing fatty acid hydroperox-
ides are preferentially hydrolyzed by phospholipase A2, with the 
peroxidized fatty acids replaced by normal fatty acids. Peroxidized 
lipids (e.g., fatty acid hydroperoxides and phospholipid-associated 
hydroperoxides) may be reduced by the glutathione peroxidase–
glutathione–glutathione reductase system (see Fig. 3-26) or by the 
peroxiredoxin–thioredoxin–thioredoxin reductase system. NADPH 
is needed to repair the reductants that are oxidized in the process.
Repair of DNA  Damage to DNA is inevitable, as it occurs spon-
taneously during replication and it is frequently targeted by elec-
trophiles and free radicals. However, nuclear DNA is remarkably 
stable, in part because it is packaged tightly in chromatin and 
because complex repair mechanisms have evolved to correct 
adverse alterations. Mitochondrial DNA, however, lacks histones 
and efficient repair mechanisms and therefore is more prone to 
damage. The contribution of DNA damage and DNA repair mecha-
nisms are central to toxicity culminating in carcinogenic and muta-
genic effects (see Chaps. 8 and 9). However, the mechanisms of 
DNA repair, or the disruption of normal DNA repair mechanisms, 
remain an important aspect of understanding general mechanisms 
of toxicity and are summarized here.
Direct Repair  Certain covalent DNA modifications are 
directly reversed by enzymes such as DNA photolyase, which 
cleaves adjacent pyrimidines dimerized by UV light. The enzyme 
uses the energy of visible light to correct damage, so its func-
tion is restricted to light-exposed cells. Minor DNA adducts, 
such as methyl groups attached to the O6 position of guanine, are 
cleaved by O6-methyguanine-DNA-methyltransferase (MGMT). 
While repairing the DNA, this alkyltransferase inactivates itself 
by transferring the adduct onto one of its cysteine residues, after 
which the enzyme is subjected to ubiquitination and proteasomal 
degradation. Thus, like glutathione, which is depleted during 
detoxification of electrophiles, MGMT is consumed during the 
repair of DNA.
Excision Repair  Base excision and nucleotide excision 
are two major mechanisms for removing damaged bases from 
DNA (see Chaps. 8 and 9). Lesions that do not distort the helix 
typically are removed by base excision, in which the altered 
base is recognized by DNA glycosylase that hydrolyzes the

Figure 3-25.   The role of heat-shock proteins (Hsps) in the unfolded protein response and the process of ubiquitination. Hsp90 and Hsp70 are central to recog-
nizing damaged or misfolded proteins. Hsp90 associates with misfolded proteins and acts as a protective chaperone, whereas binding of Hsp70 directs the mis-
folded protein for degradation through autophagy (Fig. 3-27), the unfolded protein response (Fig. 3-31), or proteasomal degradation. Proteins are marked for 
proteasomal destruction by the polyubiquitination pathway. Ubiquitin (Ub) is a small protein (8.6 kDa) that covalently links to a target protein (as a monomer 
or as a polymer of Ub units) to target it for degradation. Ubiquitination is a posttranslational protein modification that takes place in an enzymatic cascade com-
posed of three types of proteins—the E1 Ub-activating enzyme, the E2 Ub-conjugating enzyme, and the E3 Ub ligase. As shown in the figure, E1 first activates 
Ub in an ATP-consuming reaction by adenylating the terminal carboxyl group of Ub. In the subsequent transthiolation reaction, Ub is transferred from E1 to 
the catalytic SH group of E2. Thereafter, E3 binds both the target protein and the Ub-charged E2 and catalyzes the transfer of Ub from E2 to the target protein, 
forming an isopeptide bond between the terminal carboxyl group of Ub and the ε-amino group of a lysine in the substrate. Multiple rounds of ubiquitination 
generate polyubiquitin chains. In general, monoubiquitination triggers endocytosis or autophagy of the protein. Substrates that are tagged with Lys48-linked 
Ub chains are recognized and degraded by the 26S proteasome into small peptides, with the Ubs released for reuse. Proteins polyubiquitinated with Lys63-
linked Ub chains are generally not degraded but are essential components of signaling pathways, functioning as scaffolds to assemble signaling complexes.

103
N-glycosidic bond, releasing the modified base and creating an 
apurinic or apyrimidinic (AP) site in the DNA. For example, 
8-hydroxyguanosine, a major mutagenic product of oxidative 
stress, is removed from the DNA by a specific DNA glycosy-
lase. The AP site is recognized by the AP endonuclease, which 
hydrolyzes the phosphodiester bond adjacent to the abasic site. 
After its removal, the abasic sugar is replaced with the correct 
nucleotide by a DNA polymerase and is sealed in place by a 
DNA ligase.
DNA adducts are bulkier lesions, often produced by reactive 
intermediates (such as those from formaldehyde, aflatoxins, or ami-
nofluorene derivatives) and dimers caused by UV radiation. These 
adducts are removed by the nucleotide excision repair system (Yu 
et al., 2015). In this mechanism, proteins that recognize the dis-
torted double helix at the lesion unwind the DNA and excise a 
number of intact nucleotides on both sides of the lesion along with 
the one containing the adduct. The excised section of the strand is 
restored by insertion of nucleotides into the gap of the actions of 
DNA polymerase and ligase, using the complementary strand as 
a template. In contrast, lesions in the transcribed DNA strand that 
block RNA polymerase activity are removed by the transcription-
coupled repair system. This involves assembly of repair proteins to 
remove the stalled RNA polymerase before excising the damage 
and filling the gap. Resynthesis of the removed section of the strand 
is designated as unscheduled DNA synthesis. Overall, excision 
repair has a remarkably low error rate of less than one mistake in 
109 bases repaired.
PARP (poly-[ADP-ribose] polymerase) is another impor-
tant contributor to excision repair. PARP binds to damaged DNA 
caused by base damage or single-strand breaks (SSBs) and becomes 
activated. The active PARP cleaves NAD+ to use the ADP-ribose 
moiety of this cofactor for attaching long chains of polymeric ADP-
ribose to nuclear proteins, such as histones. The ADP-ribose unit 
contains two negative charges, so the poly(ADP-ribosyl)ated pro-
teins are also negatively charged, and the resultant electrorepulsive 
force between the charged proteins and DNA causes decondensa-
tion of the chromatin structure. This opens tightly packed chromatin 
and allows the repair enzymes to access and repair the damaged 
DNA. Thereafter, poly(ADP-ribose) glycohydrolase gains access to 
the nucleus from its perinuclear localization and reverses the PARP-
mediated modification of nuclear proteins.
Nonhomologous End Joining  This process repairs double-
strand breaks (DSBs) that may be formed when two SSBs occur in 
close proximity, or when DNA with SSBs undergoes replication. 
This repair system directly ligates the broken ends without the need 
for a homologous template. DSBs are recognized by the Ku protein

(a heterodimer of Ku70 and Ku80) that binds to the DNA end. 
Ku then binds and activates DNA-dependent protein kinase cata-
lytic subunit (DNA-PKcs), leading to recruitment and activation of 
DNA polymerases and DNA ligase. Although the nonhomologous 
end joining is error-prone, it can operate in any phase of the cell 
cycle and is the mechanism for DSB repair in nondividing termi-
nally differentiated cells including neurons.
Recombination (or Postreplication) Repair  Homologous 
recombination can be used to repair postreplication gaps and 
DSBs (Li and Heyer, 2008). Postreplication gaps form when exci-
sion of a bulky adduct or an intrastrand pyrimidine dimer does 
not occur before DNA replication. At replication, two homolo-
gous (“sister”) yet dissimilar DNA duplexes are formed; one has 
a large postreplication gap in its daughter strand and an intact 
duplex synthesized at the opposite leg of the replication fork. This 
intact duplex is utilized to complete the postreplication gap in the 
damaged duplex, which is accomplished by recombination of the 
appropriate strands of the two homologous duplexes. After separa-
tion, the sister duplex that originally contained the gap carries in 
its daughter strand a section originating from the parent strand of 
the intact sister, which in turn carries in its parent strand a section 
originating from the daughter strand of the damaged sister. This 
strand recombination explains the phenomenon of “sister chro-
matid exchange,” which is indicative of DNA damage corrected 
by recombinational repair. Given this mechanism, homologous 
recombination can function only after replication (in S and G2 
phases), when sister chromatid sequences are available for use as 
templates (see below), and accordingly, recombination is not an 
option in nondividing cells.
Proliferating cell nuclear antigen (PCNA) is a central player in 
postreplication repair. PCNA binds to chromatin and slides along the 
DNA strand to tether the polymerase to the DNA template. PCNA is 
a platform for a wide variety of proteins that participate in DNA rep-
lication and damage. These include the MRN complex (the trimer of 
Mre11, Rad50, and Nbs1 proteins), which binds to the DSB and is 
involved in end processing by generation of single-stranded DNA, 
and the breast cancer susceptibility proteins BRCA1 and BRCA2, 
which assist the single-stranded DNA in invading the undamaged 
template in an ATP-dependent process (Gao et al., 2017).
In general, there is a limited role for RNA in DNA repair 
processes. However, there is emerging evidence that the machin-
ery involved in miRNA formation, beyond its role in translational 
repression (discussed above), is also involved in DNA dam-
age repair (Hawley et al., 2017). In human cells, there are novel 
miRNAs that appear to be derived from the vicinity of DSBs. 
Moreover, small RNAs, termed DNA damage-induced RNAs 
(diRNAs), are generated only after DNA damage, and although 
their precise function is not established, they are required for 
the proficient repair of such damage. The role of small RNAs in 
repairing DNA damage is an ongoing area of research, and it will 
be important to determine whether, and if so how, they are directly 
involved in DNA repair.
Cellular Repair  The ability to repair or remove damaged cells 
or organelles is important for preventing cellular dysfunction. 
Autophagic removal of damaged cell organelles may be viewed as 
a universal mechanism of cellular repair, whereas clearance and 
regeneration of damaged axons is a mechanism specific for nerve 
cells. Autophagy is a lysosomal process in which intracellular sub-
strates are degraded within the lysosomal compartment. It is a type 
of cell death mechanism, but one that is unique in its characteris-
tic presence of cytoplasmic vacuoles resulting from engulfment of 
cellular components (Table 3-7). Autophagy is a constitutive and 
rapid process, and most autophagosomes are very short-lived, with 
a half-life of no more than 10 minutes. It is also distinct from apop-
tosis and necrosis in that the clearance of “debris” is an intracel-
lular process, and there is no involvement of other phagocytic cells. 
Autophagy occurs by the engulfment of cellular constituents (mac-
roautophagy), membrane uptake of smaller amounts of cytoplasm 
and organelles (microautophagy), or chaperone-mediated events 
that enable specific protein substrates to be taken into the lysosome 
for degradation. Autophagy is also an important mechanism for 
survival in the fasting state.
With respect to toxicity, autophagy is an important mechanism 
for removing misfolded proteins from cells resulting from oxida-
tive stress or ER stress. ER stress is an adaptive mechanism that is 
designed to trigger transcriptional activation of a genetic program 
that will enhance autophagy and the capacity for normal protein 
folding in the ER. However, if excessive or prolonged, ER stress 
ultimately triggers cell death, typically in the form of apoptosis. 
While autophagy serves the purpose of cell restitution in all cells, 
it is particularly important in terminally differentiated cells, such 
as neurons and myocytes, where cell renewal by cell replication is 
not possible.
Autophagic removal of damaged cell organelles is a process 
that supports their quality control, disposal, and recycling. For 
example, deletion of depolarized mitochondria occurs through 
selective autophagy or mitophagy in cells exposed to mitochon-
drial poisons, such as the protonophoric uncoupler CCCP, val-
inomycin, and paraquat (see Table 3-6). There are five stages of 
autophagy starting with the initiation stage, followed by elonga-
tion, membrane closure, maturation and finally, degradation of 
cytoplasmic cargo (Fig. 3-27). Autophagy is mediated by the 
actions of a core group of kinases and other proteins including 
(a) the un-51-like kinase (ULK) complex, (b) the class II PI3K 
complex, (c) a ubiquitin-like conjugation system that requires 
autophagy-related proteins (ATGs) such as ATG7, ATG3, and the 
ATG12-ATG 5:Atg16L complex, (d) the ATG9 transmembrane 
protein involved in vesicle trafficking, and (e) vacuole membrane 
protein 1 (VMP1) that binds with Beclin-1 (Bcl-2-interacting pro-
tein-1; Petibone et al., 2017). Removal of damaged mitochondria 
by autophagy is important not only for maintaining a functional 
mitochondrial pool but also for limiting oxidative cell damage 
and preventing apoptosis, because injured mitochondria may be 
sources of ROS and apoptotic factors, such as cyt c, Smac, and 
AIF. Autophagy also contributes to structural restitution of liver 
cells as is the case for removal of peroxisomes after exposure 
to peroxisome proliferators and elimination of lipid droplets in 
hepatic steatosis.
Regeneration of Damaged Axons  It is generally recognized 
that neurons in the central nervous system have limited capacity 
to regenerate. In contrast, axonal damage in peripheral neurons 
can be repaired by axonal regeneration, a process that requires 
macrophages and Schwann cells. Macrophages remove debris by 
phagocytosis and produce cytokines and growth factors, which 
activate Schwann cells to proliferate and transdifferentiate to facil-
itate and support axonal regrowth. Distal to the injury, Schwann 
cells play an indispensable role in promoting axonal regenera-
tion by increasing their synthesis of cell adhesion molecules (e.g., 
N-CAM), elaborating proteins for basement membrane reconstruc-
tion, and by producing an array of neurotrophic factors (e.g., brain-
derived neurotrophic factor, glial cell line–derived neurotrophic 
factor, and nerve growth factor) and their receptors (Wong et al., 
2017). While comigrating with the regenerating axon, Schwann 
cells guide neuronal regrowth and provide trophic support to the 
axon to reinnervate the target cell.

Mechanisms of Toxicity
Chapter 3
105
In contrast to the peripheral nervous system, neurons in the 
central nervous system lose their intrinsic growth capacity post-
natally, which occurs simultaneously with increased expression of 
some neuronal growth inhibitory Krüppel-like TFs, such as KLF4. 
Accordingly, damage to central neurons is irreversible but is com-
pensated for in part by the large number of reserve nerve cells 
that can take over the functions of lost neurons. For example, in 
Parkinson disease, symptoms are not observed until there is at least 
an 80% loss of nigrostriatal neurons.
Tissue Repair  In tissues with cells capable of multiplying, dam-
age is reversed by deletion of the injured cells and regeneration 
of the tissue by proliferation. However, it is important to remove 
the damaged cells, and often, apoptotic processes contribute. As 
described above, apoptosis is a controlled, orderly process in which 
dead cells are removed without inflammation. This is in contrast to 
necrosis which is a less orderly process that ends with cell debris in 
the extracellular environment. The constituents of the necrotic cells 
attract inflammatory cells, and the ensuing inflammation ampli-
fies cell injury (described below). However, apoptosis may support 
tissue restoration only for tissues that are made up of constantly 
renewing cells (e.g., the bone marrow, the respiratory and GI epi-
thelium, and the epidermis of the skin), or of conditionally divid-
ing cells (e.g., hepatic and renal parenchymal cells). The role of 
apoptosis as a tissue repair strategy is markedly lessened in organs 
containing nonreplicating and nonreplaceable cells, such as the 
neurons, cardiac muscle cells, and female germ cells, because dele-
tion of such cells, if extensive, will decrease organ function.
Proliferation: Regeneration of Tissue  Repair of tissue injury 
involves regeneration of lost cells, reconstitution of the extracellular 
matrix (ECM), and reintegration of the newly formed elements. In 
organs such as liver, kidney, and lung, various types of cells are 
involved in the process of tissue restoration. Nonparenchymal cells 
of mesenchymal origin residing in the tissue, such as resident mac-
rophages and endothelial cells, and those migrating to the site of 
injury, such as monocytes, produce factors that stimulate parenchy-
mal cells to divide and stimulate some specialized cells (e.g., the 
stellate cells in the liver) to synthesize ECM molecules.
Replacement of Lost Cells by Mitosis  After tissue injury, 
repair is frequently mediated by cell proliferation (Fig. 3-28). DNA 
synthesis is increased, and this response is detected experimen-
tally as an increase in the expression of proteins associated with 
S-phase of the cell cycle (including immunohistochemical detec-
tion of Ki-67 and PCNA or by incorporation of substrates into the 
DNA template during S-phase such as bromodeoxyuridine [BrdU] 
or 3H-thymidine). Also, mitotic figures can be seen microscopi-
cally in cells. As early as 2 to 4 hours after administration of a low 
dose of carbon tetrachloride to rats, the mitotic index in the liver 
increases dramatically, indicating that cells already in the G2 phase 
progress rapidly to mitosis (M-phase). The mitotic activity of the 
hepatocytes culminates at 36 to 48 hours, after a full transit through 
the cycle, indicating that quiescent cells residing in G0 enter and 
progress to mitosis. In the CCl4 model, mitosis of nonparenchymal 
cells occurs later, after activation and replication of parenchymal 
cells. In severe toxic liver injury, when hepatocyte replication is 
impaired (e.g., in rats dosed with high dosages of galactosamine 
or acetylaminofluorene, and in humans intoxicated with acetami-
nophen), restoration of the liver may depend on stem cell–derived 
cells, called oval cells, which are located in the terminal bile duct-
ules. These cells proliferate and differentiate into both hepatocytes

Figure 3-27.   The process of autophagy including the five major stages (initiation, elongation, membrane closure, maturation, and degradation). Autophagy 
is initiated by activation of the unc-51-like kinase (ULK) in a complex composed of ULK1, ULK2, autophagy-related proteins (ATG13), and FIP200, a 
scaffold protein. Additionally, phosphorylation of Beclin 1 is necessary for initiating the PI3 kinase complex, which is required for phagophore nucleation. 
The phagophore elongates around cytoplasmic components, including polyubiquitinated cargo, after which it closes to form the autophagosome. When the 
autophagosome fuses with a lysosome, the autolysosome is formed, after which the autophagosome inner membrane and its cytoplasmic cargo are degraded by 
acid proteases and hydrolases. The signal transduction pathways along with the cycle from initiation to degradation is illustrated. Abbreviations: PE, phospha-

Unit I
and biliary epithelial cells (cholangiocytes) (Fausto et al., 2006; 
Shin and Kaestner, 2014). As these hepatic progenitor cells produce 
α-fetoprotein, the increase in serum α-fetoprotein is predictive of 
an improved outcome of acetaminophen-induced injury.
Hepatic sinusoidal endothelial cells originate from bone mar-
row progenitors, and when they are chemically injured (e.g., by 
monocrotaline) bone marrow–derived precursor cells move into 
the sinusoids and contribute to their replacement, as demonstrated 
in a rat model of hepatic sinusoidal obstruction syndrome (Harb 
et al., 2009). Accordingly, bone marrow infusion ameliorates, 
whereas myelosuppression aggravates, such liver injury. In an 
ozone-exposed lung, the nonciliated, bronchiolar Clara cells and 
alveolar type II pneumocytes proliferate and differentiate to replace 
damaged ciliated bronchial epithelial cells and type I pneumocytes, 
respectively (Tam et al., 2011). In some tissues, such as intestinal 
mucosa and bone marrow, stem cells first divide to provide self-
renewal and then differentiate to replace more mature cells lost 
through injury.
The liver is uniquely capable of regeneration, and it can fully 
regenerate in a matter of days after partial hepatectomy. In this 
model, there is a pattern of gene expression that programs this 
regeneration. Genes that are upregulated include those that code 
for TFs important in proliferative signaling, such as c-fos, c-jun, 
Egr1, and c-myc (see Fig. 3-13), the antiapoptotic protein Bcl-XL, 
and mdm2, a ubiquitin ligase that limits p53-dependent inhibition 
of the cell cycle (see Fig. 3-33). Some genes involved in cell cycle 
inhibition are temporarily overexpressed (e.g., the cyclin-dependent 
kinase inhibitor p21 and gadd45), providing an inhibitory signal 
that keeps tissue regeneration precisely regulated. Nevertheless, 
gene expression is reprogrammed so that DNA synthesis and mito-
sis gain priority over specialized cellular activities. For example, as 
a result of dedifferentiation, regenerating hepatocytes have reduced 
levels of cytochrome P450, some phase II conjugating enzymes, 
and PPARα. Similarly, hepatic stellate cells showed decreased 
accumulation of fat and vitamin A.
The regenerative process is initiated by the release of numer-
ous chemical mediators from damaged cells. Nonparenchymal 
cells, such as resident macrophages and endothelial cells, release 
cytokines and growth factors that promote and propagate the 
regenerative process. In the partial hepatectomy model, the ini-
tial or priming phase of liver regeneration is controlled by TNF-α 
and IL-6, whose hepatic mRNA and serum levels increase. TNF, 
secreted from Kupffer cells, acts on the macrophages in an auto-
crine manner, activating its receptor and the coupled signal trans-
duction network. This in turn activates NF-κB, which increases 
IL-6 expression. Secreted IL-6 exerts its action on the hepatocytes 
and through its receptor (Fig. 3-13) activates Janus kinase (JAK) 
and induces TFs (e.g., Stat3 and C/EBPβ) to activate several down-
stream target genes. This cytokine network promotes transition of 
the quiescent liver cells (G0) into cell cycle (G1) and makes them 
receptive to growth factors (“priming”). Growth factors, especially 
hepatocyte growth factor (HGF), transforming growth factor-α 
(TGF-α), and heparin-binding epidermal growth factor–like growth 
factor (HB-EGF), initiate the progression of the primed cells in the 
cycle toward mitosis (Fausto et al., 2006; Michalopoulos, 2013). 
Despite its name, neither the formation nor the action of HGF is 
restricted to the liver. It is produced by resident macrophages and 
endothelial cells of various organs, including liver, lung, and kid-
ney, and in a paracrine manner activates receptors on neighboring 
parenchymal cells.
The communication between parenchymal and nonparenchy-
mal cells during tissue repair is mutual. For example, transform-
ing growth factor-α (TGF-α), a mitogen produced by regenerating 
hepatocytes, acts as an autocrine and a paracrine mediator on liver 
cells as well as on adjacent nonparenchymal cells. The mitogenic 
growth factors initiate signaling through the MAPK pathway and 
the PI3K–Akt pathway (Fig. 3-13), thereby activating numerous 
TFs (including c-Jun, c-Fos, c-Myc, FoxM1B, NF-κB, and Stat3), 
which ultimately induce cyclins and the protein phosphatase cdc25. 
Cyclins also activate Cdks by associating with them (Fig. 3-28), 
whereas cdc25 activates Cdk1 and Cdk2 by dephosphorylating two 
amino acid residues (Thr14 and Tyr15). The activated Cdks accel-
erate the cell cycle mainly by phosphorylation of retinoblastoma 
protein (pRb), which when phosphorylated is inactivated to enable 
cell cycle progression.
Although the regulation of proliferation by cytokines and 
growth factors is considered an essential part in the repair of most 
tissues, there are also tissue-specific features of repair. For example, 
in the liver, bile acids stimulate hepatocyte proliferation through 
activation of FXR (Huang et al., 2006). In mice subjected to partial 
hepatectomy, liver regrowth is accelerated by bile acid feeding, but 
is markedly delayed when FXR (Table 3-3) is deleted or recircula-
tion of bile acids from the intestine to the liver is prevented.
In addition to proliferation, cell migration also contributes 
to tissue repair. For example, when the mucosa of the GI tract is 
damaged, cells of the residual epithelium rapidly migrate to the 
site of injury where they elongate to reestablish the continuity of

Figure 3-28.   The cell division cycle and participating cyclins and cyclin-
dependent protein kinases. Most cells are normally in G0 phase, a differenti-
ated and quiescent state. After receiving signals to divide, they progress into 
the G1 phase of the cell division cycle. G0/G1 transition involves activation 
of immediate early genes for cells to acquire replicative competence, and 
the cells progress to the phase of DNA synthesis (S). After DNA replication, 
cells prepare further for mitosis in the G2 phase. Mitosis (M) is the shortest 
phase of the cell cycle (approximately 40 minutes out of the 40-hour-long 
cycle of hepatocytes) and most likely requires the largest energy expendi-
ture per unit of time. The daughter cells produced may differentiate and 
enter the pool of quiescent cells (G0), substituting for those which had been 
lost. Also illustrated are the cyclins and cyclin-dependent protein kinases 
(Cdk) that are required for DNA replication and cell division.

107
the surface even before this can be achieved by cell replication. 
Cell movement involves orderly dissociation of cadherin-mediated 
cell–cell contacts (involving β-catenin phosphorylation) and inte-
grin-mediated cell–ECM contacts at focal adhesions (involving 
phosphorylation of focal adhesion kinase [FAK]), assembly of actin 
stress fibers, and formation of lamellopodia.
Mucosal repair is dictated not only by growth factors and cyto-
kines operative in tissue repair elsewhere but also by trefoil factors 
(TFFs). TFFs are small (7 to 12 kDa) protease-resistant proteins that 
are abundantly secreted from specific mucosal cells (e.g., intestinal 
goblet cells) and are associated with the mucous layer of the GI tract 
(Aihara et al., 2017). TFF expression is rapidly upregulated at the 
margins of mucosal injury by cytokine and growth factor signaling. 
Some growth factors (e.g., HGF and EGF) influence motility (moto-
gens) and proliferation (mitogens) of enterocytes, whereas TFFs are 
potent motogens. Although TFFs do not act alone, they are the only 
peptides shown to be essential for restitution of the injured intestinal 
mucosa. For example, dextran sodium sulfate (DSS) causes marked 
and diffuse colonic mucosal injury, but in TFF-3 null mice, an other-
wise nonlethal dose of DSS elicits severe toxicity that can be lethal.
Replacement of the Extracellular Matrix  The ECM is a bio-
chemical and structural support composed of a variety of proteins 
(such as collagen and elastin), proteoglycans (such as chondroitin 
sulfate), and glycosaminoglycans (such as hyaluronic acid). Each 
tissue has its own specific ECM composition, but in liver, stellate 
cells located in the space of Disse are major contributors to ECM 
production. Stellate cells store vitamin A and are generally at rest. 
However, these cells are activated during liver regeneration, when 
they undergo mitosis and transdifferentiate into myofibroblast-like 
contractile and secretory cells (Tsuchida and Friedman, 2017). 
Activation of stellate cells is mediated chiefly by two growth fac-
tors, namely platelet-derived growth factor (PDGF) and TGF-β. 
Both growth factors may be released from platelets which accumu-
late and degranulate at sites of injury, and TGF-β is also secreted 
from activated stellate cells. TGF-β is a major regulator of stel-
late cell transdifferentiation and stimulates the synthesis of ECM 
components and α-smooth muscle actin. As previously described, 
TGF-β acts through its Ser/Thr kinase receptor (Fig. 3-14), which 
phosphorylates the TFs Smad2 and 3 (Fabregat et al., 2016). 
TGF-β also plays a central role in ECM formation in other tissues. 
In kidney and lung, for example, TGF-β targets the mesangial cells 
and the septal fibroblasts, respectively. Remodeling of the ECM 
is aided by matrix metalloproteinases, which hydrolyze specific 
components of the matrix, as well as by tissue inhibitors of matrix 
metalloproteinases.
The way in which tissue regeneration is terminated after repair 
is unclear, but the gradual dominance of TGF-β, a potent anti-
mitogen, is a contributing factor in the termination of cell prolif-
eration. Additionally, ECM production may be halted by induction 
of Smad7 (an inhibitory Smad) (Fig. 3-14), which competitively 
inhibits phosphorylation of Smad2 and 3. However, although repair 
of the ECM helps to regenerate injured tissue, chronic stimulation 
by TGF-β also plays a role in fibrosis (discussed below).
Other Reactions to Tissue Injury  In addition to mediators that 
aid in the replacement of lost cells and the ECM, resident mac-
rophages and endothelial cells activated by cell injury produce 
other mediators that induce ancillary reactions with uncertain ben-
efit or harm to tissues. Such reactions include inflammation, altered 
production of acute-phase proteins, and generalized reactions such 
as fever.
Inflammation—Leukocyte 
Invasion  Alteration of the 
microcirculation and accumulation of inflammatory cells are the 
hallmarks of inflammation. These processes are largely initiated 
by resident macrophages secreting cytokines, such as TNF-α 
and interleukin-1 (IL-1), which increase capillary permeability 
through release of mediators from endothelial cells and fibroblasts 
(Brenner et al., 2013). In the face of toxicity, activated endothe-
lial cells facilitate the egress of circulating leukocytes into the 
injured tissue by releasing chemoattractants and simultaneously 
express selectins, a group of cell adhesion molecules that inter-
act with ligands on the surface of leukocytes, causing them to 
“roll” onto the capillary surface to facilitate transmigration into 
the damaged tissue. A strong interaction develops between inter-
cellular adhesion molecules (ICAM-1) expressed on endothelial 
cell membranes and integrins expressed on leukocyte membranes. 
This interaction is also essential for the subsequent transendothe-
lial migration of leukocytes. Chemoattractants involved in this 
response include cytokines (or chemokines), such as the MCP-1 
and IL-8, as well as lipid-derived compounds, such as platelet-
activating factor (PAF) and leukotriene B4 (LTB4). Ultimately, all 
types of cells near the injury express ICAM-1, thus promoting leu-
kocyte invasion, and the invading leukocytes synthesize additional 
chemotactic mediators to propagate the inflammatory response. 
The major regulators of this entire response include the proinflam-
matory mediators TNF-α and IL-1, which activate NF-κB and C/
EBP (Brenner et al., 2013).
Inflammation: ROS and RNS Production  Macrophages, as 
well as leukocytes, recruited to the site of injury undergo a respira-
tory burst, discharging free radicals and enzymes into the damaged 
tissue. The respiratory burst is a multifactorial and robust produc-
tion of ROS in the inflamed tissue, with NADPH oxidase (NOX2) 
responsible for the production of superoxide anion from NADPH 
to increase ROS (El-Benna et al., 2016). The superoxide anion 
generated by NOX2 activity can give rise to the hydroxyl radical 
(HO•) through the Fenton reaction. Moreover, nitric oxide synthase 
(NOS) also contributes to the respiratory burst by catalyzing the 
formation of the nitric oxide free radical (•NO) via oxidation of 
arginine to citrulline. Finally, myeloperoxidase (MPO) in granu-
locytes catalyzes for the formation of HOCl, a powerful oxidizing 
agent, from HOOH and chloride ion. Like HOOH, HOCl can also 
form HO• resulting from electron transfer from Fe2+ or from O2
•  to 
HOCl. All these reactive intermediates exert beneficial antimicro-
bial activity at the site of microbial invasion, but because they are 
so reactive, they can also damage the adjacent healthy tissues and 
thus contribute to propagation of tissue injury.
In some chemical-induced injuries, inflammation plays the 
leading role. For example, alpha-naphthylisothiocyanate (ANIT), 
a cholestatic chemical, causes neutrophil-dependent hepatocellu-
lar damage. ANIT acts on bile duct epithelial cells, causing them 
to release chemoattractants for neutrophils, which on invading the 
liver, injure hepatocytes. Kupffer cell activation, TNF release, and 
subsequent inflammation are also prominent and causative events in 
galactosamine-induced liver injury in rats (Stachlewitz et al., 1999).
Altered Protein Synthesis: Acute-Phase Proteins  Interleukins, 
including IL-6, IL-1, and IL-8 along with TNF-α, alter the tran-
scriptional activity of genes encoding a group of proteins referred 
to as acute-phase reactants, and these can be increased or decreased 
(positive and negative acute-phase proteins, respectively; Table 3-8). 
Many of the hepatic acute-phase proteins, such as C-reactive pro-
tein and hepcidin, are secreted into the circulation, where elevated 
levels in serum are diagnostic of tissue injury, inflammation, or 
neoplasm. Increased sedimentation of red blood cells, which is also 
indicative of these conditions, is due to enrichment of blood plasma 
with positive acute-phase proteins such as fibrinogen.
Apart from their diagnostic value, positive acute-phase pro-
teins may play roles in minimizing tissue injury and facilitating

108
General Principles of Toxicology
Unit I
repair. For example, many of them, such as α2-macroglobulin 
and α1-antiprotease, inhibit lysosomal proteases released from 
the injured cells and recruited leukocytes. Haptoglobin binds 
hemoglobin in blood, MT complexes metals in cells, heme 
oxygenase oxidizes heme to biliverdin, and opsonins facilitate 
phagocytosis. Upregulated acute-phase proteins may also be 
involved in the clearance of substances released on tissue injury. 
For example, induction of hepcidin triggers degradation of the 
iron efflux channel to decrease duodenal Fe2+ absorption and 
Fe2+ export from macrophages and which limits systemic avail-
ability of iron.
Downregulated acute-phase proteins include some plasma pro-
teins, such as albumin, transthyretin, and transferrin. Furthermore, 
hepatic enzymes (e.g., several forms of cytochrome P450 and glu-
tathione S-transferases), ligand-activated TFs (e.g., PPARα and the 
bile acid receptor FXR), and transporters such as bile acid trans-
porters on sinusoidal and canalicular membrane of hepatocytes and 
the bile canalicular export pump Mrp2 are also downregulated. The 
downregulation of hepatic metabolizing enzymes and transporters 
often alters the disposition and toxicity of bile acids and xenobiotics 
during the acute phase of tissue injury.
Although the acute-phase response is phylogenetically pre-
served, some of the acute-phase proteins are somewhat species 
specific. For example, during the acute phase of tissue injury or 
inflammation, C-reactive protein and serum amyloid A levels dra-
matically increase in humans but not in rats, whereas the concen-
trations of α1-acid glycoprotein and α2-macroglobulin increase 
markedly in rats but only moderately in humans.
Mechanisms of Adaptation
Adaptation is a biological process by which an organism devel-
ops increased tolerance to the harm itself. It involves responses 
acting to preserve or regain the biological homeostasis in the face 
of increased harm. Theoretically, adaptation to toxicity may result 
from biological changes causing (1) diminished delivery of the caus-
ative chemical(s) to the target, (2) decreased size or susceptibility 
of the target, (3) increased capacity of the organism to repair itself, 
and (4) mechanisms to compensate for the toxicant-inflicted dys-
function. Mechanistically, adaptation involves sensing the noxious 
chemical and/or the initial damage or dysfunction, with a response 
that limits the toxicity that typically occurs through altered gene 
expression. Such mechanisms are briefly outlined here.
Adaptation by Decreasing Delivery to the Target  Given 
that the first step in the development of toxicity is delivery of the 
ultimate toxicant to the target, some adaptive processes include 
those that decrease absorption, increase sequestration by intracel-
lular binding proteins, enhance their detoxification, or promote cel-
lular export.
Regulation of Iron Absorption  Adaptive mechanisms triggered 
by iron itself regulate intestinal absorption of this essential yet 
potentially harmful metal ion, with absorption adjusted to meet 
demand. Fe2+ is taken up from the intestinal lumen into entero-
cytes by the proton-coupled divalent metal transporter 1 (DMT1) 
localized in the luminal membrane, and it is exported across the 
basolateral membrane of these cells through the ferroportin iron

109
channel. High iron intake diminishes expression of both DMT1 and 
ferroportin in the enterocytes, whereas low intake has the opposite 
effect. DMT1 is regulated at the translational level by the intra-
cellular iron-regulatory proteins (IRPs) that contain a [4Fe-4S] 
cluster. Iron supply influences the Fe content of this cluster and in 
turn affects the capacity of IRP to bind to mRNAs containing the 
iron response element (IRE). In iron deficiency, loss of Fe from the 
[4Fe-4S] cluster allows the IRP1 apoprotein to bind to and protect 
DMT1 mRNA from enzymatic degradation and increase DMT1 
protein. Conversely, iron overload saturates IRP1 with Fe so that it 
cannot bind to and stabilize DMT1 mRNA, which decreases DMT1 
and limits Fe2+ absorption. DMT1 also mediates the transport of 
Cd2+ such that its overexpression in conditions of iron deficiency 
can also increase intestinal absorption of this toxic metal ion.
In addition to its uptake, Fe homeostasis is maintained by fer-
roportin, which allows efflux of iron from enterocytes and other 
cells that may store iron including macrophages (from phagocyto-
sis) and hepatocytes. Ferroportin in plasma membranes is regulated 
directly by the acute-phase reactant hepcidin (Table 3-8; Ganz, 
2011), which binds to ferroportin and triggers its internalization 
and lysosomal hydrolysis. Hepcidin is also regulated by iron supply, 
as iron deficiency decreases hepcidin expression, precluding fer-
roportin degradation, and in concert with upregulated DMT1, per-
mits increased iron absorption from the duodenum. In contrast, iron 
overload increases the secretion of hepcidin to increase degradation 
of ferroportin, and in concert with decreased synthesis of DMT1, 
shuts down iron absorption from the intestine. This system can also 
respond to toxicants. For example, the hepatotoxic herbicide diquat 
undergoes redox cycling and increases hepatic free-iron concentra-
tions. However, an adaptive increase in hepatic hepcidin mRNA 
decreases plasma iron levels (Higuchi et al., 2011) to provide an 
appropriate protective response.
Induction of Ferritin and Metallothionein  Adaptive cellular 
accumulation of the binding proteins ferritin and metallothionein 
(MT) is protective against iron and cadmium ions, respectively. 
Like DMT1, ferritin is regulated by IRP1 in the face of iron over-
load. However, apoIRP1 acts oppositely on ferritin mRNA to block 
its translation. Therefore, in iron overload, ferritin mRNA is mark-
edly increased. In this manner, ferritin is protective as it acts to bind 
and remove Fe2+ systemically.
MT is induced by cadmium, and elevated levels of MT protect 
the liver by restricting distribution of this toxic metal ion to sensitive 
intracellular targets. Induction of MT by Cd2+ is likely mediated by 
displacement of Zn ions from the protein which then activate metal-
responsive transcription factor 1 (MTF-1) to increase transcription 
of the MT gene. The adaptive response afforded by MT induction 
is illustrated by the fact that induction of the metal binding protein 
subsequently protects against highly toxic exposures to Cd2+.
The Electrophile Stress Response: Induction of Detoxification  
Adaptive increases in detoxification and cellular export have a 
major role in limiting toxicity. An important contributor to such 
adaptation is the Keap1-Nrf2 pathway, a cytosolic complex that 
senses thiol reactivity. The response is initiated by Nrf2 (NF-E2-
related factor-2), a TF that activates genes with an electrophile 
response element (EpRE) in their regulatory region (Fig. 3-29; 
Chartoumpekis et al., 2015). Normally Nrf2 is retained in the cyto-
plasm by Keap1, a cysteine-rich homodimeric protein. Keap1 keeps 
Nrf2 inactive by anchoring it to the cytoskeleton and maintains low 
intracellular levels by linking it to cullin 3, a component of E3 
ubiquitin ligase that directs it to proteasomal degradation. Many 
different thiol-sensitive compounds bind to Keap1 at its reactive 
cysteine thiol groups thereby forcing Keap1 to release Nrf2. This 
broad group of activators includes electrophiles, α,β-unsaturated 
aldehydes and ketones, isothiocyanates, thiol-reactive metal ions, 
and direct and indirect oxidants. On disruption of the Keap1–Nrf2 
complex, active Nrf2 translocates into the nucleus, forms a heter-
odimer with small Maf proteins, and activates genes through bind-
ing to EpREs.
There are many genes with EpRE motifs. The broad response 
is illustrated in Fig. 3-29 and includes (1) enzymes that detox-
ify xenobiotics, (2) enzymes that eliminate O2
• , (3) proteins that 
detoxify heme, (4) enzymes involved in the synthesis of GSH and 
its regeneration from GSSG, and (5) efflux transporters. It should 
be noted that the list of Nrf2-regulated genes provided here is an 
incomplete list, and other Nrf2-induced proteins of known toxico-
logical relevance are discussed later.
To provide an example of the broad, pleiotropic response 
initiated by Nrf2 activation, Fig. 3-30 illustrates Nrf2-mediated 
adaptive changes that facilitate elimination and detoxification 
of electrophilic chemicals in response to t-butylhydroquinone 
(tBHQ), a well-known inducer of this pathway. Additional com-
pounds that induce Nrf2 through thiol reactivity include butylated 
hydroxyanisole (BHA), sulforaphane (a compound in broccoli), 
curcumin (a compound in turmeric-containing 2 α,β-unsaturated 
ketone groups), resveratrol (a hydroquinone-type compound in red 
grapes), oltipraz (a dithiolenethione compound), and derivatives 
of oleanolic acid, such as 2-cyano-3,12-dioxooleana-1,9(11)-dien-
28-oic acid (CDDO). These chemicals have been shown to protect 
from toxicant-induced tissue injury and cancer. For example, treat-
ment with oleanolic acid or CDDO ameliorates acetaminophen-
induced hepatotoxicity and aflatoxin-induced hepatocarcinogenesis 
(Klaassen and Reisman, 2010). Moreover, CDDO has been evalu-
ated clinically for use in chronic kidney disease, and there is grow-
ing interest in Nrf2 activation in cancer chemoprevention (Cao 
et al., 2015).
Finally, the critical role of Nrf2 in adapting to toxicity is 
confirmed in Nrf2 knockout mice that are more sensitive to 
many toxicants including the hepatotoxicity of acetaminophen, 
the pulmonary toxicity of butylated hydroxytoluene or cigarette 
smoke, the neurotoxicity of 3-nitropropionic acid, and the car-
cinogenicity of benzo[a]pyrene (Klaassen and Reisman, 2010). 
Conversely, liver-specific deletion of Keap1 constitutively activates 
Nrf2 in hepatocytes, causing them to overexpress many detoxi-
fying enzymes and become resistant to acetaminophen-induced 
hepatotoxicity.
Adaptation by Decreasing the Target Density or 
Responsiveness  Decreasing the density and sensitivity of the 
xenobiotic target is an adaptation mechanism for several recep-
tors. Such alterations underlie the tolerance induced by opioids and 
drugs of abuse. There are several opioid receptors, but the major 
receptor for drugs like morphine or heroine is the µ-opioid receptor. 
This is a Gi protein–coupled inhibitory receptor, and stimulation 
by an agonist inhibits adenyl cyclase to decrease levels of cyclic 
AMP levels and PKA activity. Brief stimulation induces adapta-
tion manifested as receptor downregulation by loss of the receptor 
from the plasma membrane. Receptor downregulation is mediated 
by receptor internalization that facilitates lysosomal degradation 
rather than recycling back to the membrane (Bailey and Connor, 
2005). Additionally, there is an opening of K+ channels in neurons 
with opioid receptors to cause hyperpolarization. Tolerance to opi-
oids leads to the requirement for increasing doses of a drug such 
as morphine to maintain pharmacologic activity, and the need for 
higher or more frequent doses enhances abuse potential.

Unit I
Adaptation by Increasing Repair  There are several repair 
mechanisms that can be induced after toxicant exposure. Some of 
these may aid in repairing damaged molecules, proteins, and DNA, 
others support regeneration of injured tissue.
The Electrophile Stress Response: Induction of Enzymes 
Repairing Oxidized Protein  In addition to enzymes that enhance 
detoxification, Nrf2 activation induces proteins that mediate pro-
tein repair. These include thioredoxin 1 (Trx1) and thioredoxin 
reductase 1 (TR1), which catalyze reduction of oxidized proteins, 
and several subunits of the proteasome complex. Trx1 and TR1 are 
also reduction partners for ribonucleotide reductase, thereby sup-
porting de novo synthesis of deoxyribonucleotides necessary for 
DNA synthesis. In this manner, induction of Trx1 and TR1 mediate 
repair of both protein and DNA.
Induction of Chaperones Repairing Misfolded Protein: The 
Heat-Shock Response  The role of heat-shock proteins has been 
previously described in the section “Repair of Proteins.” Regulation 
of these proteins to support adaptive responses is mediated by heat-
shock transcription factors (HSF), mainly HSF1, which transactivate 
genes that encode Hsp through heat-shock response elements (HSE). 
This process often takes place simultaneously with the electrophile 
response discussed above. HSF1, like Nrf2, normally resides in the 
cytoplasm, where it associates with Hsp90, Hsp70, and Hsp40. On 
heat- or chemical-induced protein damage, Hsps are sequestered 
by damaged proteins (Fig. 3-25), allowing HSF1 to translocate to 
the nucleus, where it forms a trimer and is phosphorylated. The 
chaperones Hsp90 and Hsp70, together with co-chaperone pro-
teins, are especially important in maintaining the integrity of hun-
dreds of proteins (Schopf et al., 2017) including those carrying out 
housekeeping functions and those involved in signaling and apop-
tosis. Therefore, induction of Hsps has pleiotropic effects beyond 
increased protection from cytotoxicity.
Endoplasmic Reticulum Stress and the Unfolded Protein 
Response  The endoplasmic reticulum (ER) is the primary site 
where transmembrane and secretory proteins are folded. In this 
Ca2+-rich, oxidative environment, protein modifications including 
N-glycosylation at asparagine residues, formation of disulfide bonds, 
and folding assisted by EPR-resident chaperones occur. When 
there is damage to proteins being processed in the EPR by reactive 
metabolites (often produced in situ by CYP enzymes), generation of

lular level by interacting with Keap1 that promotes its ubiquitination and proteasomal degradation. Electrophiles covalently bind to, whereas oxidants oxidize, 
the reactive thiol groups of Keap1, causing Keap1 to release Nrf2. Alternatively, Nrf2 release may follow its phosphorylation by protein kinases. After being 
released from Keap1, active Nrf2 accumulates in the cell, translocates into the nucleus, and forms a heterodimer with small Maf proteins to activate genes that 
contain electrophile response element (EpRE) in their promoter region. These include enzymes, binding proteins, and transporters functioning in detoxication 
and elimination of xenobiotics, ROS, and endogenous reactive chemicals, as well as some proteins that can repair or eliminate oxidized proteins (highlighted 
in blue). Induction of such proteins represents an electrophile stress response that provides protection against a wide range of toxicants. Abbreviations: AR,

111
ROS, and/or depletion of Ca2+ in the EPR lumen, there is accumula-
tion of unfolded or misfolded proteins, a condition known as EPR 
stress (Malhi and Kaufman, 2011; Carreras-Sureda et al., 2017). 
EPR stress can be triggered by high secretory activity, along with 
conditions that perturb protein maturation, calcium homeostasis, or 
redox balance. Additionally, it can also be induced by compounds 
that disturb different aspects of EPR homeostasis, including hap-
sigargin (which blocks SERCA-mediated Ca2+ uptake into EPR; 
see Table 3-6), tunicamycin (which inhibits N-glycosylation of 
proteins), castanospermine (which interferes with maturation of 
nascent glycoproteins by deglucosylation), and brefeldin A (which 
inhibits transport of proteins from EPR to Golgi). When the load of 
unfolded or misfolded proteins in the EPR exceeds the capacity of 
EPR-resident chaperones, these proteins trigger a complex adaptive 
response called unfolded protein response (UPR) that is designed to 
reduce the load of unfolded proteins and restore EPR homeostasis 
(Fig. 3-31). However, if EPR homeostasis cannot be restored, UPR 
switches to a proapoptotic pathway that eliminates damaged cells.
In the adaptive mode, UPR includes mechanisms to (1) reduce 
mRNA translation to decrease the functional load on the EPR, 
(2) increase transcription of EPR chaperones to boost the folding 
capacity, and (3) remove aberrant proteins via translocon peptide 
channels into the cytosol for proteasomal degradation. As noted 
above, the final option is to eliminate the affected cell via apoptosis 
if EPR stress is sustained or massive. The eukaryotic translation ini-
tiation factor 2α (eIF2α) and TF X-box binding protein 1 (XBP1), 
ATF6, ATF4, and C/EBP homologous protein (CHOP; also called 
Gadd153) are the major regulators of this adaptive response.
There are three transmembrane EPR proteins that sense the 
overload of the EPR lumen with misfolded proteins and transduce

enzymes, exporters, and repair proteins) that, when induced in response to the tBHQ-mediated electrophile stress, facilitate the detoxication and export of 
tBHQ and/or its metabolites, the detoxication of ROS formed during the biotransformation of tBHQ, and repair or removal of proteins damaged by the reactive 
metabolites. Proteins induced by Nrf2 are marked with an asterisk. tBHQ (designated as 1 in the aromatic ring) can be detoxified by glucuronidation (2), but 
cytochrome P450–catalyzed dehydrogenation to t-butylquinone (tBQ; 3), which contains electrophilic carbon atoms (+), is believed to be the actual inducer of 
electrophile stress response. tBQ can be detoxified by conjugation with glutathione (GSH) to form tBHQ-SG (4); it can covalently bind to SH groups in proteins 
(5), including Keap1, or by accepting an electron (e), tBQ can also form the t-butylsemiquinone radical (6), which passes the electron to molecular oxygen to 
form superoxide anion radical (O2
•), completing a redox cycle. In a process catalyzed by superoxide dismutase (SOD), O2
• can form HOOH (left side of figure), 
which is detoxified by GSH-peroxidase (GPX), or involved in the Fe2+-catalyzed Fenton reaction to form hydroxyl radical (HO•).

General Principles of Toxicology
Unit I
this signal to initiate the UPR (Fig. 3-31). These include PKR-like 
ER kinase (PERK), inositol-requiring enzyme 1α (IRE1α), a pro-
tein with kinase and endoribonuclease (RNase) activities, and the 
precursor form ATF6 (activating TF 6). Under normal conditions, 
the EPR-resident soluble chaperone BiP (or Grp78) inhibits UPR. 
However, when stressed, the unfolded proteins accumulating in the 
lumen of the EPR sequester BiP away from these sensors, thereby 
turning them on to signal for the UPR. PERK catalyzes the phos-
phorylation of the eIF2α, resulting in attenuation of translation and 
decreased synthesis of most (but not all) proteins. Upon activation, 
IRE1α undergoes dimerization and trans-autophosphorylation, 
which increases its RNase activity, enabling it to cleave and process 
the mRNA for XBP1 (X-box binding protein-1), and XBP1 upregu-
lates numerous genes involved in secretory function including ER 
chaperones and lipid synthesis. The third sensor ATF6 resides as 
a 90-kDa precursor protein in the EPR with its Golgi localization 
sequences masked by BiP. On dissociation from BiP, ATF6 trans-
locates to the Golgi where it is cleaved by SP1 and SP2 proteases 
(site-1 and site-2 proteases). This cleavage releases activating factor 
6 (ATF6), a potent TF that together with XBP1 activates transcrip-
tion of ER chaperone proteins that promote protein folding.
Overall, UPR serves to sustain cell survival. However, with 
excessive EPR stress, PERK, IRE1α, and ATF6 act in concert to 
initiate apoptosis (Carreras-Sureda et al., 2017). Mitochondria-
associated membranes (MAMs) are specialized membranes of the 
ER that are in close proximity to the mitochondrial outer membrane 
and facilitate communication between the EPR and mitochon-
dria. Core components of UPR, including PERK and IRE1α, are 
enriched in MAMs. Moreover, while PERK-catalyzed phosphoryla-
tion of eIF2α decreases the synthesis of most proteins, it can initiate 
cell death programs by complex regulation that ultimately induces 
the expression of the proapoptotic protein Bim along with the cell 
surface death receptor TRAIL-2, while inhibiting the expression of 
the antiapoptotic protein Bcl-2. Activated IRE1α may also initiate 
cell death by recruiting the adaptor protein TNF receptor–associ-
ated factor-2 (TRAF2), with subsequent activation of apoptosis 
signal-regulating kinase 1 (ASK1) and JNK. After phosphoryla-
tion, JNK activates proapoptotic Bim and inactivates antiapoptotic 
Bcl-2 proteins (Malhi and Kaufman, 2011). The IRE1α–TRAF2 
complex may also recruit and activate procaspase 12. Alternatively, 
Ca2+ released from the EPR can activate calpains, which cleave 
the EPR-associated procaspase-12 into active caspase-12. The latter 
then engages the caspase cascade by activating effector caspases to 
initiate apoptosis.
Induction of Enzymes Repairing DNA: The DNA Damage 
Response  Maintaining the fidelity of DNA is critical to cell sur-
vival, and DNA damage initiates numerous pathways for repair 
(discussed above). The tumor suppressor p53, regarded as the 
“guardian of the genome,” is also critical to DNA repair processes. 
Normally, p53 is kept inactive and at low levels in the cytoplasm 
by binding protein mdm2 (mouse double minute homolog 2), an 
E3 ubiquitin ligase that ubiquitinates p53 and facilitates its pro-
teasomal degradation. However, it is activated by several kinase 
pathways that signal in response to double-stranded DNA breaks. 
These pathways include the MRN complex (the trimer of Mre11,

immunoglobulin protein), a Hsp70 molecular chaperone, inhibits UPR. However, under endoplasmic reticulum (ER) stress, BiP dissociates from the UPR 
transducers to chaperone misfolded proteins into the ER lumen and initiate one of three branches of the UPR. Activating factor 6 (ATF6) translocates to the 
Golgi where it is processed to generate a transcription factor that targets genes involved in ER-associated protein degradation (ERAD). When activated, IRE1α 
forms an oligomer that processes the mRNA coding for X-box binding protein 1 (XBP1), another transcription factor that activates proteins involved in ERAD,

2α), which represses global translation while facilitating the activation of numerous factors including activating transcription factor 4 (ATF4). Activation of 
ATF4 drives the expression of genes involved in protein folding, amino acid biosynthesis, oxidative stress, autophagy, and apoptosis. (From Malhi H, Kaufman

and Ku80; see the section “Repair of DNA”) that bind and acti-
