and DNA-dependent protein kinase catalytic subunit (DNA-PKcs), 
respectively, to phosphorylate and activate p53 (Batchelor et al., 
2009). With DNA damage and through the cascade that phospho-
rylates p53, it escapes from mdm2, allowing its activation, stabili-
zation, and translocation to the nucleus. p53 facilitates DNA repair 
by several mechanisms. First, it initiates cell cycle arrest at the G1/S 
interface by upregulating the cyclin-dependent kinase inhibitor 
protein p21, thereby allowing more time for DNA repair. Second, 
it increases expression of numerous proteins directly involved in 
DNA repair. These include gadd45 (growth arrest and DNA dam-
age inducible) that interacts with histones and facilitates access of 
proteins (e.g., topoisomerase) to DNA, XPE, and XPC, members 
of the xeroderma pigmentosum group of proteins important in 
UV-induced DNA damage recognition, MSH2 (operating in mis-
match repair), PCNA to enable excision and the recombination 
repair processes, and ribonucleotide reductase to synthesize deoxy-
ribonucleotides needed to support DNA synthesis. Beyond their 
action on p53, the DNA damage–activated protein kinases (ATM, 
ATR, and DNA-PK) also phosphorylate histone H2AX adjacent to 
the damage, which then becomes ubiquitinated. These markings in 
turn recruit repair proteins (including BRCA1) and induce chro-
matin relaxation for better access of repair enzymes to the lesion.
Overall, DNA repair mechanisms and the more complicated 
DNA damage response (DDR) protect normal cells from DNA 
damage and potential transformation to cancer cells. However, 
these mechanisms may also provide a selective growth advantage 
by protecting cells from DNA damage associated with radiotherapy 
or chemotherapy, and contribute to an adaptive response that causes 
resistance to anticancer treatments (discussed below).
Adaptive Increase in Proliferative Responses to Promote 
Tissue Repair  In association with toxic injury, there is often a 
resulting signaling cascade to initiate tissue repair via cell replace-
ment. In this situation, phosphatases such as PTP and PTEN, 
which normally serve as brakes on growth factor receptor-initiated 
mitogenic signaling, play an active role (refer to Fig. 3-13). These 
enzymes, which contain reactive cysteine thiols in their active sites, 
are covalently modified and thereby inactivated by electrophiles. 
In this manner, the loss of their inhibitory activity on proliferative 
signaling cascades serves to amplify intracellular signaling for 
mitosis and survival.
NF-κB is also a converging pathway for proliferative signal-
ing, as it transactivates genes producing cell cycle acceleration (e.g., 
cyclin D1 and c-Myc) and inhibitors of apoptosis (e.g., antiapop-
totic Bcl proteins and the caspase IAPs; Karin, 2006). In addition, 
NF-κB also transactivates ferritin, GST, SOD1, HO-1, a protea-
some subunit, and gadd45, facilitating detoxification and molecular 
repair. Collectively, these roles of NF-κB explain its broad involve-
ment in tolerance to chemically induced tissue injury and adaptation 
to ionizing radiation.
Proliferating cells also need to increase protein synthesis, a pro-
cess controlled by the protein kinase mTOR (mammalian target of 
rapamycin). mTOR is a major regulator of general metabolic activity. 
It is activated by the MAPK pathway through phosphorylation of the 
MAPK isoform Erk and by the PI3K pathway through phosphory-
lation of Akt (see Fig. 3-13). These pathways are also activated in 
response to oxidant or electrophile exposure as they are held in check 
by controlled PTPs and PTEN (Fig. 3-13) such that inhibition of 
these phosphatases also increases signaling through Erk and Akt. In 
the cascade, Erk and Akt activate mTOR that in turn phosphorylates 
and regulates effectors of protein synthesis, such as the translation 
repressor protein 4EBP1 and the protein kinase S6K, which modi-
fies ribosomes increasing their translational efficiency (Shaw and 
Cantley, 2006). In contrast to its activation during cell proliferation, 
it is shutdown in conditions such as hypoxia to reduce ATP synthesis.
Adaptation to Hypoxia: The Hypoxia Response  Hypoxia-
inducible factor 1α (HIF-1α) is maintained at very low constitu-
tive intracellular levels because of continuous hydroxylation of its 
two proline residues by HIF-prolyl hydroxylases. Hydroxylation of 
the proline residues permits a ubiquitin ligase subunit (von Hippel 
Lindau protein [VHL]) to initiate proteasomal degradation. Under 
normal, non-stress conditions, HIF-1α is a short-lived protein with 
a half-life of less than 5 minutes. HIF-1α is also kept transcrip-
tionally inactive by hydroxylation at one of its asparagine residues 
by HIF-asparagine hydroxylases, which prevents interaction of 
HIF-1α with transcriptional coactivators, such as p300 and CREB-
binding protein (CBP). However, when O2 delivery is impaired and 
hypoxia persists for more than a few minutes, HIF-1α increases 
markedly (Ju et al., 2016). Moreover, HIF hydroxylases have a 
KM for O2 that is very close to ambient O2 concentration, so that 
when the O2 concentration falls, there is decreased hydroxylation 
of HIF-1α as well as its VHL-mediated ubiquitination for protea-
somal degradation, which increase the abundance and transcrip-
tional activity of HIF-1α. HIF hydroxylases are members of the 
Fe2+ and ascorbate-dependent dioxygenases and Fe2+ deficiency 
also impairs their activity. This feature explains why iron chela-
tors (deferoxamine) or Fe2+-mimicking metal ions (Co2+ and Ni2+) 
induce and activate HIF-1α (Maxwell and Salnikow, 2004).
In hypoxic conditions, HIF-1α dimerizes with HIF-1β (also 
called Arnt, which is the dimerization partner for AhR; Fig. 3-11). 
The HIF complex transactivates a vast array of genes containing 
the hypoxia response element (HRE). Many of the activated genes 
function to enable acclimatization to hypoxia (Harada, 2016). These 
include (1) erythropoietin (EPO) produced largely in kidney to acti-
vate erythropoiesis in bone marrow, (2) proteins involved in iron 
homeostasis (e.g., transferrin, TFR, ceruloplasmin, and heme oxy-
genase) to increase availability of iron for erythropoiesis, (3) vascular 
endothelial growth factor (VEGF) and angiopoietin-2 to stimulate 
blood vessel growth, (4) proteins facilitating glycolysis including the 
glucose transporter GLUT1 and some glycolytic enzymes, (5) pro-
teins that correct acidosis caused by glycolytic overproduction of lac-
tate (e.g., a monocarboxylate transporter and a Na+/H+ exchanger for 
export of lactate and H+), (6) the REDD1 signal transducer protein 
that initiates a complex signaling pathway that leads to suspension of 
the ATP-consuming protein synthesis via inactivation of mTOR, and 
(7) a generalized response to promote ECM remodeling (e.g., matrix 
metalloproteinase-2) and cell migration and to induce autophagy and/
or apoptosis. As an example of an adaptive response to hypoxia, it has 
been shown that in mice housed in a low O2 environment (hypoxic 
preconditioning), HIF-1α is stabilized in the retina to induce hypoxia-
responsive genes (EPO and VEGF) upon which the retina becomes 
resistant to light toxicity (Grimm et al., 2005). Adaptation to hypoxia 
also occurs in response to high-altitude conditions, chronic cardio-
respiratory dysfunction, and ischemic preconditioning. The hypoxia 
response can develop as a result of toxicities that cause acute or sub-
acute conditions of hypoxia (e.g., respiratory muscle weakness after 
organophosphate intoxication and diquat-induced pulmonary injury) 
or as a delayed sequel to conditions of respiratory surface restriction 
as is seen in hard metal disease (see Chap. 15).
The Energy Stress Response to Adapt to Energy 
Depletion  Cells try to maintain ATP levels at all times. When 
the rephosphorylation of AMP and ADP to ATP does not keep 
up with the rate of ATP use, the ratio of AMP to ATP increases.

114
General Principles of Toxicology
Unit I
AMP-activated protein kinase (AMPK) represents an adaptive 
cellular mechanism that responds to energy deficits to boost ATP 
production and limit ATP consumption (Hardie et al., 2006). The 
kinase is strongly activated by AMP, and it is phosphorylated by 
protein kinase LKB1 (or by the calmodulin-dependent protein 
kinase kinase [CaMKK] in neurons). Phosphorylated AMPK is 
activated and targets two sets of proteins that function to increase 
ATP production either from catabolism of glucose and fatty acids 
or by promoting mitochondrial ATP biogenesis, while concurrently 
reducing reactions that consume ATP. For example, to increase 
ATP levels AMPK increases (a) glucose uptake (via glucose trans-
porters GLUT4 and GLUT1), (b) glycolysis (via phosphorylation 
and activation of 6-phosphofructo-2-kinase [PFK-2]), and (c) fatty 
acid oxidation in mitochondria (via phosphorylation and inactiva-
tion of acetyl-CoA-carboxylase). Simultaneously, it induces an 
extensive program to reduce ATP consumption by broadly inhib-
iting (a) glycogen synthesis by phosphorylation and inactivation 
of glycogen synthase, (b) lipid synthesis by phosphorylating and 
inactivating acetyl-CoA-carboxylase, (c) cholesterol synthesis by 
phosphorylating and inactivating HMG-CoA reductase, (d) glu-
cose synthesis via decreased expression of key gluconeogenetic 
enzymes such as phosphoenolpyruvate carboxykinase (PEPCK) 
and glucose-6-phosphatase, and (e) protein synthesis by inhibiting 
mTOR. AMPK-mediated modulation of cellular energy supply and 
consumption occurs largely through kinase-mediated phosphoryla-
tion reactions rather than transcriptional regulation, rendering this 
pathway a rapid, adaptive response.
In addition to the rapid AMPK-dependent reprogramming 
of cellular intermediary metabolism, there is an ultimate means 
for cells to generate fuel for ATP production in case of nutrient 
shortage. This slower process involves consumption of the cell’s 
own constituents (lipid droplets, glycogen particles, proteins, and 
organelles) by nonselective or bulk autophagy (Rabinowitz and 
White, 2010; Petibone et al., 2017) with lysosomal hydrolysis pro-
viding essential metabolic intermediates (amino acids, fatty acids, 
nucleosides, and carbohydrates). As discussed earlier, autophagy is 
a five-step process that involves a complex program of kinase activ-
ity including ULK-1, autophagy-related proteins (Atg), and focal 
adhesion kinase family-interacting protein of 200 kDa (FIP200), 
which is an initiator of autophagosome formation (Fig. 3-27). 
When nutrients are abundant, mTOR phosphorylates and inacti-
vates ULK-1 and Atg13, thereby inhibiting autophagy. However, 
in conditions of nutrient shortage, mTOR is inactivated by signal-
ing pathways, such as those activated by AMP and hypoxia, which 
releases inhibition of the ULK-1–Atg13–FIP200 complex, and in 
turn initiates autophagosome formation (Hosokawa et al., 2009). 
The important role that mTOR serves in switching autophagy on 
and off is further evidence of its role as key regulator of cellular 
energy homeostasis.
In surveying the major cellular adaptation mechanisms to 
toxicants herein, it should be easy to recognize that one toxic 
event may initiate several of these adaptive responses. For exam-
ple, cells exposed to a hypoxic environment can rapidly respond 
with both AMPK-mediated program of energy stabilization and 
HIF-1α-directed adaptation to oxygen shortage. Theoretically, an 
electrophilic toxicant that binds covalently to cellular macromol-
ecules can also generate oxidative stress, and would be expected 
to induce numerous adaptive processes, including the electrophile 
response, the heat-shock response, EPR stress, the DDR, and cell 
proliferation. Furthermore, if severe enough to compromise ATP 
synthesis, the energy stress response may be elicited. Such com-
plexity illustrates the broad underpinning of almost any mecha-
nism of toxicity.
Adaptation by Neurohumoral Mechanisms  There are numerous 
adaptive responses to dysfunctions of organs or organ systems that 
are mediated by humoral or neuronal signals between cells located 
in the same or different organs. For example, the rapid hyperventi-
lation evoked by acute hypoxia or HCN inhalation is mediated by 
a neural reflex initiated by glomus cells in the carotid body. These 
chemosensitive cells generate a Ca2+ signal via AMPK (Evans 
et al., 2005). In addition, CN−, mitochondrial electron transport 
inhibitors (e.g., rotenone, antimycin A, myxothiazole), uncouplers 
(e.g., 2,4-dinitrophenol), or ATP-synthase inhibitors (e.g., oligomy-
cin) as well as AMPK activators mimic the response to hypoxia in 
these cells.
Final and Irreversible Actions of Repair 
and Adaptation Failure
The most important consequences of inappropriate repair are irre-
versible organ failure, as is the case with necrosis or tissue changes 
that advance to fibrosis or carcinogenesis. Before discussing these 
final, irreversible effects, general principles concerning failure to 
repair or adapt are presented.
When Repair Fails  Although repair mechanisms operate at 
molecular, cellular, and tissue levels, they can fail to provide pro-
tection against injury. Toxicity is manifested when repair of the 
initial injury fails because the repair mechanisms become over-
whelmed, exhausted, or impaired. Failure occurs because the 
fidelity of the repair mechanisms is not absolute, as in the case 
for DNA repair. However, repair most often fails when damage 
overwhelms repair mechanisms, by depleting protective elements 
and exhausting the capacity for repair. For example, alkylation of 
DNA depletes MGMT, lipid peroxidation depletes α-tocopherol, 
and overproduction of oxidized or otherwise damaged proteins can 
exhaust the pool of ubiquitin. Sometimes toxicant-induced injury 
adversely affects the repair process itself. For example, ethanol 
generates ROS via CYP2E1 that impairs the proteasomal removal 
of damaged proteins. Autophagic removal of cell constituents may 
be compromised by lysosomotropic drugs that increase intralyso-
somal pH to reduce lysosomal hydrolase activity, a defect that may 
contribute to the mechanism of chloroquine-induced myopathy in 
rats. Thiol-reactive chemicals can inactivate ubiquitin-activating 
E1, E2, and E3 enzymes because each family contains a catalyti-
cally active cysteine, as well as lysosomal cysteine proteases (e.g., 
cathepsins B, H, and L), to compromise the clearance of damaged 
proteins by both the UPS and autophagy. These repair mechanisms 
are especially important in neurons, as genetic deletion of proteins 
mediating UPS and autophagy causes accumulation of intraneuro-
nal inclusions, neuronal loss, or neurodegeneration. In fact, there is 
growing evidence that human neurodegenerative diseases appear 
to be directly related to dysfunction of UPS and/or autophagy 
(Bedford et al., 2011; Menzies et al., 2017).
It is also possible that repair contributes to toxicity. This often 
occurs in a passive manner, when excessive amounts of NAD+ are 
cleaved by PARP in the process of repairing damaged DNA strands, 
or when too much NAD(P)H is consumed for the repair of oxidized 
proteins and endogenous reductants. Either event can compromise 
oxidative phosphorylation, which is also dependent on the supply of 
reduced cofactors, and exacerbate ATP depletion. However, repair 
also may play an active role in toxicity. This is often observed with 
chronic tissue injury, when repair processes progress to uncon-
trolled proliferation rather than remodeling. Such proliferation of 
cells may become neoplastic, whereas overproduction of ECM 
progresses to fibrosis. A specific case is when the cellular repair 
processes that normally degrade damaged proteins, such as the UPS

Mechanisms of Toxicity
Chapter 3
115
and the autophagy pathway, become inappropriately stimulated by 
adverse signaling, causing degradation of ordinary intracellular 
proteins. For example, glucocorticoids can cause dose- and time-
dependent muscle atrophy and wasting. The mechanism underlying 
muscle atrophy is reduced signaling through the IGF-1–PI3K–Akt 
pathways, which leaves FoxO TFs unchecked to regulate expression 
of muscle-specific ubiquitin ligases (MAFbx, also called atrogin-1, 
and MuRF1) that enhance muscle catabolism and wasting (Atherton 
et al., 2016).
When Adaptation Fails  As described in the previous sections, 
moderate oxidative stress activates NF-κB, AP-1, and Nrf2 to ini-
tiate adaptive protection. However, extensive oxidant exposure 
aborts these programs because it leads to oxidation of thiol groups 
in the DNA-binding domain of these TFs. Similarly, some other-
wise adaptive and protective mechanisms may be harmful under 
extreme conditions and numerous examples are provided here. 
For example, tubuloglomerular feedback is important in regulat-
ing glomerular filtration. However, with acute renal injury, tubular 
reabsorption is impaired and causes polyuria, which triggers tubu-
loglomerular feedback to further reduce glomerular blood flow and 
filtration. These seemingly protective events may precipitate anuric 
renal failure. It is also possible that an adaptive mechanism that is 
beneficial in the short term may become harmful when forced to 
operate for a prolonged time. For example, bulk autophagy likely 
contributes to the lethal wasting syndrome induced by TCDD. 
Chronic inflammation, tissue injury, or cancer may lead to iron 
deficiency and anemia because IL-6 upregulates hepcidin secre-
tion from the liver, which reduces intestinal iron absorption. As 
discussed earlier, NF-κB activation is indispensable for repair via 
proliferation of the acutely injured tissue. However, NF-κB also 
targets cytokines (e.g., TNF and IL-1β), which in turn further acti-
vate NF-κB through receptor signaling (see Fig. 3-13). This vicious 
cycle may lead to chronic inflammation and cancer when persistent 
tissue injury maintains NF-κB signaling (Karin, 2006). Sustained 
cell proliferation is causally related to neoplasia in a variety of 
models including phenobarbital-induced liver tumors in rodents 
and lung tumors associated with occupational exposure to silica. 
Sustained activation of HIF-1α in tumors facilitates invasiveness, 
in part by increasing VEGF expression and angiogenesis. In the 
kidney, HIF-1α may also be involved in fibrogenesis, as it targets 
critical genes that maintain the extracellular matrix (ECM) such as 
tissue inhibitor of metalloproteinase-1 (TIMP-1).
Toxicity Resulting from Inappropriate 
Repair and Adaptation
Failure to repair involves molecular, cellular, and tissue dysfunc-
tion and can show species- and age-related differences. Some tox-
icities involve dysrepair at an isolated level. For example, hypoxia 
and dyspnea develop after exposure to metHb-forming chemicals if 
the amount of metHb produced overwhelms the capacity of metHb 
reductase. Because this repair enzyme is deficient at early ages, 
neonates are especially sensitive to chemicals that cause methemo-
globinemia. Ineffective reduction of metHb also contributes to the 
formation of Heinz bodies, which are protein aggregates formed 
in red blood cells as a function of oxidative stress and aging. As 
noted earlier, there are also species differences in metHb reductase 
activity that are responsible for differences in sensitivity to metHb 
formation.
Tissue Necrosis  Although cell death ensues when repair mecha-
nisms are inefficient or the molecular damage is not readily revers-
ible, progression to tissue necrosis can be interrupted by apoptosis 
and cell proliferation. Injured cells can initiate apoptosis, which 
counteracts the progression of the toxic injury. Apoptosis does this 
by preventing necrosis of injured cells and the consequent inflam-
matory response, which may cause additional injury by releasing 
cytotoxic mediators. A classic example illustrating the role of the 
inflammatory response in tissue necrosis is activation of Kupffer 
cells with administration of bacterial LPS (endotoxin) which 
greatly exacerbates the hepatotoxicity of galactosamine. In contrast, 
when the Kupffer cells are selectively eliminated by pretreatment 
of rats with gadolinium chloride, the necrotic effect of numerous 
hepatotoxicants is markedly alleviated (Nolan, 2010). Blockade 
of Kupffer cell function with glycine also protects the liver from 
numerous hepatotoxicants and is associated with decreased levels 
of pro-inflammatory cytokines (TNF-α) and increased levels of 
anti-inflammatory mediators such as IL-10 (Bruck et al., 2003).
Cell proliferation also serves to negate the loss of organ func-
tion when necrosis occurs. Early cell division is instrumental in the 
rapid and complete restoration of injured tissue and the prevention 
of necrosis. For example, if cell proliferation is blocked in liver, a 
normally non-necrogenic dose of a toxicant such as carbon tetra-
chloride causes hepatic necrosis (Mehendale, 2005).
The dose–response relationship that describes toxicant-
induced injury also contributes to the dose response for irrevers-
ible injury. In general, tissue repair increases up to a threshold dose 
that can limit or prevent irreversible injury. However, once that 
threshold is reached, toxicity and cell death progress rapidly. For 
example, with hepatotoxicants, apoptosis and cell proliferation are 
operative with latent tissue injury caused by low (non-necrogenic) 
doses of toxicants, but are inhibited with severe injury induced by 
high (necrogenic) doses. For example, 1,1-dichloroethylene, car-
bon tetrachloride, and thioacetamide induce apoptosis in the liver 
at low doses, but cause hepatic necrosis after high-dose exposure. 
Similarly, there is an early mitotic response in the liver to low-dose 
carbon tetrachloride, but this response is not observed with higher 
doses of the solvent.
Fibrosis
Fibrosis is characterized by excessive formation and deposition of 
excess connective tissue with abnormal composition of the ECM. 
In general, fibrotic conditions can develop in any organ, but those 
of note include liver, lung, kidney, and heart. Hepatic fibrosis, or 
cirrhosis, is associated with fatty liver disease as well as toxicants 
including chronic consumption of ethanol, treatment with metho-
trexate, and exposure to thioacetamide, carbon tetrachloride, and 
high levels of iron. Chronic fibrotic changes in the renal glomer-
ulus are associated with diabetic kidney disease, and pulmonary 
fibrosis is induced by drugs such as bleomycin and amiodarone 
and associated with prolonged inhalation of oxygen or mineral 
particles. Finally, drugs including doxorubicin may cause general-
ized cardiac fibrosis, whereas drugs acting as agonists on 5-HT2B 
receptors, such as fenfluramine, bromocriptine, and methysergide, 
cause valvular interstitial cell proliferation and heart valve fibro-
sis. Exposure to high doses of ionizing radiation induces fibrosis 
in many organs.
Physiologically, fibrosis is an exaggerated wound healing 
response which interferes with normal organ architecture and func-
tion. The epithelial-mesenchymal transition (EMT) is a fundamen-
tal process in fibrotic disease. This response is characterized by the 
phenotypic transformation of an epithelial cell into a fibroblast or 
myofibroblast. Although EMT is necessary for organ development 
and wound healing, it is uncontrolled in fibrosis, and cells that man-
ufacture the ECM during tissue repair overproduce the matrix in an 
uncontrolled manner.

116
General Principles of Toxicology
Unit I
As an end-stage disease, fibrosis is detrimental because (1) 
scars can compress and ultimately obliterate parenchymal cells and 
blood vessels; (2) deposition of basement membrane components 
between the capillary endothelial cells and the parenchymal cells 
presents a diffusional barrier that contributes to malnutrition of the 
tissue cells; (3) an increased amount of ECM alters the elasticity 
and flexibility of the whole tissue, compromising the mechanical 
function of organs such as the heart and lungs, and (4) fibrotic dis-
ease can change basic aspects of cell behavior, including cell polar-
ity, motility, and gene expression.
TGF-β is a central mediator of fibrotic disease. Its role as 
a mediator of fibrosis is well recognized, but additional factors 
including connective tissue growth factor (CTGF), platelet-derived 
growth factor (PDGF), and IL-4 also contribute to the process. 
TGF-β is highly overexpressed in several experimental models of 
fibrosis and in patients with active liver cirrhosis. Moreover, there 
is experimental evidence that subcutaneous injection of TGF-β 
induces local fibrosis, and transgenic mice engineered to overex-
press TGF-β develop severe hepatic fibrosis.
The fibrotic action of TGF-β is due to increased production and 
decreased degradation of ECM components. TGF-β stimulates the 
synthesis of individual ECM components in target cells via the Smad 
pathway (see Fig. 3-14). TGF-β also augments ECM formation by 
inhibiting ECM degradation by increasing the expression of inhibitor 
proteins that antagonize ECM-degrading enzymes, such as TIMP-1 
and plasminogen activator inhibitor-1 (PAI-1). TGF-β also induces 
transcription of its own gene in target cells, suggesting that the TGF-β 
produced by these cells can amplify in an autocrine manner the pro-
duction of the ECM (Fabregat et al., 2016). There are also changes 
to the composition of the ECM in fibrotic diseases. Basement mem-
brane components, such as collagen IV and laminin, as well as the 
fibrillar-type collagens (collagens I and III), which confer rigidity to 
tissues, increase disproportionately during fibrogenesis.
In general, miRNAs contribute to fibrosis by affecting the 
EMT, and numerous miRNAs contribute to this process. Three 
major miRNAs involved in fibrotic disease are miR-21, miR-29, 
and miR-200, which are directly regulated by TGF-β activation 
of pSmad3 (Zou et al., 2017). Constitutively, miR-21 is highly 
expressed in the healthy kidney, but it is markedly overexpressed 
in idiopathic pulmonary fibrosis (IPF) and renal fibrosis. Major 
targets for miR-21 include matrix metalloprotease-9 (MMP-9) and 
Smad-7, and downregulation of these targets prevents ECM deg-
radation and inhibition of TGF-β signaling, respectively (Loboda 
et al., 2016). miR-29, which acts to repress collagen translation, is 
downregulated by TGF-β and in doing so, α-smooth muscle actin 
and collagen I are increased, particularly in the kidney (Roderburg 
et al., 2011). miR-200 is expressed in lung fibroblasts and is down-
regulated in fibrotic conditions. Decreased expression of miR-200 
exerts a profibrotic action by increasing expression of numerous 
mesenchymal markers.
Carcinogenesis
As there is an entire chapter dedicated to carcinogenesis (see Chap. 8), 
only those aspects relating directly to mechanisms associated with 
repair and adaptive processes are discussed here. A central feature 
of carcinogenic mechanisms is altered expression of proteins that 
function as proto-oncogenes and/or suppressor proteins. These 
important proteins can be altered by mutation resulting from direct 
DNA damage or by altered transcription leading to overexpression 
of proto-oncogenes or decreased expression of tumor suppressors.
Another important consideration is that genetic or epigene-
tic mechanisms are involved in carcinogenesis. DNA-reactive or 
genotoxic mechanisms bring about a qualitative change in gene 
expression, with expression of an altered gene product, a mutant 
protein or miRNA, and gain or loss in activity. In contrast, epigen-
etic mechanisms are non-DNA reactive (or non-genotoxic) and do 
not alter gene sequence, but rather, cause quantitative change in 
gene expression resulting in more or less gene product. Regardless 
of mechanism, cancer ultimately develops from cellular failures in 
apoptosis and/or from uncontrolled cell proliferation.
Genotoxic Mechanisms of Carcinogenesis  Chemicals that react 
with DNA may cause damage such as adduct formation, oxidative 
alteration, and strand breakage. As described above, these lesions 
are repaired or the injured cells are eliminated. If neither event 
occurs, a lesion in the parental DNA strand may induce a mutation 
in the daughter strand during replication. The mutation may remain 
silent if it does not alter the protein encoded by the mutant gene or if 
the mutation causes an amino acid substitution that does not affect 
the function of the protein. Alternatively, the genetic alteration may 
be incompatible with cell survival. The worst scenario occurs when 
the altered genes express mutant proteins that reprogram cells for 
multiplication and escaping apoptosis (i.e., immortalization). This 
trait is passed to daughter cells during mitosis, enabling sustained 
proliferation. Moreover, enhanced DNA replication and cell divi-
sion increases the likelihood of additional mutations that may fur-
ther augment their growth advantage. Ultimately, tumors arise from 
the nidus of rapidly proliferating transformed cells (Fig. 3-32).
The critical role of DNA repair in preventing carcinogenesis 
is exemplified by the human heritable disease xeroderma pigmen-
tosum (XP) in which affected individuals lack excision repair pro-
teins. These people are at high risk for developing sunlight-induced 
skin cancers, and they are also more prone to chemically induced 
DNA damage from recognized carcinogens including aflatoxin B1, 
aromatic amines, and polycyclic hydrocarbons (Giordano et al., 
2016).
Mutation of Proto-oncogenes  Proto-oncogenes are highly 
conserved genes that encode proteins that stimulate the progres-
sion of cells through the cell cycle or oppose apoptosis (Jonckheere 
et al., 2017). Under normal conditions, these important proteins are 
required for regulating growth, with essential roles in supporting 
embryogenesis, tissue regeneration, and growth factor responses. 
Mutations in proto-oncogenes can cause permanent activation and/
or overexpression of these proteins to favor neoplastic transforma-
tion. The altered gene is then referred to as an oncogene. Fig. 3-33 
depicts several proto-oncogene products that are involved in initiat-
ing the cell division cycle.
An example of mutational activation of an oncogene is the 
Ras family of proteins (H-Ras, K-Ras, and N-Ras). Ras proteins 
are G-coupled proteins with GTP/GDP-binding capacity as well 
as GTPase activity. Ras is activated downstream from growth fac-
tor receptors and protein tyrosine kinases and upstream from the 
mitogen-activated protein kinase (MAPK) cascade (Figs. 3-12 and 
3-13). Ras functions as a molecular switch that is only active in the 
GTP-bound form. Mutations in Ras genes occur mainly in codons 
12, 13, or 61 and represent a dominant gain of function because they 
lock the protein in the permanently active GTP-bound form to drive 
uncontrolled cell division and transformation (Fig. 3-33). Activating 
mutations in Ras genes occur in approximately 20% of all human 
cancers, with K-Ras mutations being the most prevalent (Chang 
et al., 2016). Mutation of the K-ras oncogene is also mechanisti-
cally linked to pancreatic cancer (Jonckheere et al., 2017).
Activating mutations in the proto-oncogene b-Raf is another 
important example of genotoxic mechanisms of carcinogenesis. 
Raf proteins are protein kinases just downstream from Ras and 
are the first signal transducers in the MAP kinase pathway (see 
Fig. 3-12). These mutations are expressed in 15% to 30% of all

Mechanisms of Toxicity
Chapter 3
117
human tumors, and are particularly prevalent in malignant mela-
noma (with a frequency of nearly 60% of these tumors). The most 
common mutation (V600E) occurs within the activation segment of 
b-Raf. The increased kinase activity of the mutant b-Raf continu-
ally sends a Ras-independent proliferative signal down the MAPK 
pathway to promote neoplastic transformation (Roskoski, 2010).
While constitutive activation of oncogenes is a common initia-
tor of chemical carcinogenesis, overexpression resulting from gene 
amplification also can contribute to neoplastic cell transformation. 
Such an event can occur spontaneously or may be initiated by DNA 
strand breaks.
Mutation of Tumor Suppressor Genes  Tumor suppres-
sor genes encode proteins that inhibit the cell cycle progression 
or promote DNA repair or apoptosis when there is DNA damage. 
Fig. 3-33 depicts such proteins, which include cyclin-dependent 
protein kinase inhibitors (e.g., p15, p16, and p21), TFs including 
p53 and Smads, ATM and ATR that sense DNA damage to signal 
p53 activation, BRCA1 and BRCA2 that contribute to recombi-
national DNA repair, PTEN phosphatase that turns off the PI3K–
Akt pathway-mediated proliferative signaling, and the tuberous 
sclerosis complex-2 (TSC2) that prevents activation of mTOR. 
Uncontrolled proliferation occurs when the mutant tumor suppres-
sor gene can no longer suppress cell division. Inactivating mutations 
of specific tumor suppressor genes in germ cells are responsible 
for the inherited predisposition to cancer, as in familial retinoblas-
toma (pRb), Wilms tumor, familial polyposis, and Li–Fraumeni 
syndrome. Mutations of tumor suppressor genes in somatic cells 
contribute to nonhereditary cancers, with p53, p16, PTEN, and pRb 
showing high frequency of mutation in human cancer.
As described earlier, p53, otherwise referred to as the guard-
ian of the genome, controls cell cycle arrest to enable DNA repair 
(Fig. 3-34). The critical and essential role of p53 function is further 
illustrated in conditions in which it is deficient or defective. Cells 
that have no p53 are about a million times more likely to permit 
DNA amplification than are cells with a normal level of this sup-
pressor gene. Furthermore, mice with the p53 gene deleted develop 
tumors in 6 to 9 months. Mutations in the p53 gene are found in 
approximately 50% of human tumors and in a variety of induced 
cancers. The majority are missense mutations that alter amino 
acid sequence to yield an aberrant that cannot activate the essen-
tial tumor suppressor transcriptional response (Joerger and Fersht, 
2016). A classic example of carcinogen-mediated p53 mutation 
is the point mutation in codon 249 that predominates in human 
hepatocellular carcinomas arising from exposure to aflatoxin B1. 
There is compelling evidence that this mutation is causally related 
to aflatoxin-induced liver cancer in humans (Hussain et al., 2007; 
Wogan et al., 2012). However, this same mutation is not observed 
in rat liver following aflatoxin exposure.
The contribution of miRNA regulation to cancer is complex 
(Wang and Taniguchi, 2013). For example, p53 promotes the process-
ing of numerous miRNAs and specifically upregulates transcription 
of miRNA34a-c after DNA damage. The miRNA34 family regulates 
the G1/S cell cycle checkpoint, so in the presence of p53 mutation, 
this important checkpoint in cell cycle control is lost. Furthermore, 
direct damage to genes coding for miRNAs may also contribute to 
carcinogenesis. For example, amplification of miR-21, which nor-
mally represses the translation of PTEN, and deletion of miR-15 and 
miR-16, which normally represses translation of cdc25A, occur in 
spontaneous human malignancies. Loss of the repressive function of 
these miRNAs serves to disrupt cell cycle regulation.
Non-genotoxic Mechanisms in Carcinogenesis  Non-genotoxic 
carcinogens often stimulate sustained cell proliferation. This can 
occur by direct activity on proliferative signaling, such as PKC 
activation by phorbol esters or by protein phosphatase inhibition 
by okadaic acid. Additionally, sustained cell proliferation can be 
evoked by endogenous mitogens and hormones, including estro-
gens on mammary gland or liver cells, TSH on the follicular cells 
of the thyroid gland, and luteinizing hormone on Leydig cells in 
testes, and some toxicants that cause sustained cell injury (such 
as chloroform and d-limonene). There are also numerous xeno-
biotics, often microsomal enzyme inducers, that cause sustained 
proliferation, particularly in liver, to cause tumors in rodents but 
not in humans. Such compounds include phenobarbital and DDT 
along with peroxisome proliferators (e.g., fibrates, Wy-14643, and 
dihalogenated and trihalogenated acetic acids).
Role of Signaling in Carcinogenesis  As described under tis-
sue repair, cell injury evokes the release of mitogenic growth factors 
such as HGF and TGF-α from tissue macrophages and endothe-
lial cells. Although these growth factors are instrumental in tissue 
repair after acute cell injury, their continuous presence is poten-
tially harmful because they may ultimately transform the affected 
cells into neoplastic cells. Indeed, transgenic mice that overexpress 
TGF-α develop hepatomegaly at a young age and tumors by 12 
months (Fausto et al., 2006).
Role of DNA Methylation and Histone Modification in 
Carcinogenesis  Some non-genotoxic carcinogens alter DNA 
methylation. As discussed earlier, specific cytosine residues located

Unit I
in CpG islands in promoter regions can be methylated by DNA 
cytosine methyltransferases using S-adenosyl methionine as the 
methyl donor. Promoter methylation silences genes by weakening 
TF binding to the promoter and triggering secondary alterations in 
histone proteins, which renders the histone more compact, thereby 
reducing access of TFs and other proteins involved in transcrip-
tion initiation at the gene promoter. It is well documented that the 
normal methylation pattern of DNA is disrupted in cancer cells. 
Paradoxically, global DNA hypomethylation is observed in the 
face of hypermethylation of promoter regions of numerous tumor 
suppressor genes (Esteller, 2005), such as Cdk inhibitors (p15 and 
p16), the DNA repair enzyme MGMT, and the lipid phosphatase 
PTEN. Importantly, both global hypomethylation and tumor sup-
pressor gene hypermethylation intensify with increased malignancy 
of the tumor. The relevance of hypermethylation-induced silencing 
of tumor suppressor genes in carcinogenesis is supported by the 
finding that inhibitors of DNMTs (such as 5-aza-2′-deoxycytidine) 
can stop the growth of cancer cells and induce their differentiation 
by demethylating the dormant tumor suppressor genes to restore 
their expression (Esteller, 2005).
DNA methylation status can also be altered by non-geno-
toxic carcinogens. This is the case for ethionine, which depletes 
S-adenosyl-methionine (the methyl donor for DNMTs), and dietha-
nolamine, which inhibits cellular utilization of choline, a dietary 
source for methyl groups needed to convert homocysteine to methi-
onine. Additionally, on prolonged administration, the methylation 
status of Ras or Raf is altered by phenobarbital, whereas meth-
ylation of c-Jun, c-Myc, and IGF-2 is affected by treatment with 
dichloroacetic acid, dibromoacetic acid, or trichloroacetic acid.
Role of MicroRNAs in Non-genotoxic Mechanisms of 
Carcinogenesis  miRNAs can affect carcinogenic mechanisms 
through effects on DNA repair (described above) as well on

Figure 3-33.   Key regulatory proteins controlling the cell division cycle along with relevant effects in the signaling cascades that regulate the cell cycle. The 
left side of the figure highlights proteins that accelerate the cell cycle and are oncogenic if permanently active or expressed at high level. In contrast, proteins 
on the right, represented by blue symbols, decelerate or arrest the cell cycle to suppress oncogenesis, unless they are inactivated (e.g., by mutation). Expression 
of cyclin D (cD) is increased, for example, by growth factors signaling through Ras proteins and the MAPK pathway as well as by Wnt and Hedgehog (Hh)

pathways considered to be non-genotoxic. The miR-17-92 cluster 
is considered oncogenic because its members promote mitosis by 
repressing the translation of PTEN and p21, and they inhibit apop-
tosis by repressing the proapoptotic protein Bim. miR-29b also tar-
gets the mRNAs of p21 and Bim, and miR-221/222 targets PTEN, 
TIMP-3 (metalloproteinase inhibitor 3), and Bim (Garofalo and 
Croce, 2011). The role of miRNAs in tumorigenesis induced by 
non-genotoxic carcinogens is well exemplified by PPARα-induced 
rodent hepatocarcinogenesis. In this example, treatment with 
Wy-14643 downregulates let-7c miRNA, which normally represses 
translation of c-Myc. Accordingly, the reduced levels of let-7c in 
response to Wy-14,643 increases c-Myc protein synthesis, which 
drives hepatocellular proliferation. Furthermore, c-Myc upregu-
lates the miR-17-92 cluster which in turn represses the translation 
of p21, an antimitotic cyclin-dependent kinase, and Bim, a pro-
apoptotic protein, and together, these actions permit mitosis while 
opposing apoptosis. In contrast to normal mice, PPARα-humanized 
mice (expressing the human PPARα) do not show downregulation 
of let-7c or hepatocellular proliferation with Wy-14,643 treatment, 
supporting a causative relationship between these events (Gonzalez 
and Shah, 2008).
Genotoxic and non-genotoxic mechanisms of carcinogenesis 
may not be mutually exclusive, but rather, they can complement 
or amplify each other. Increased mitotic activity, regardless of 
mechanism or pathway involved, increases the likelihood of car-
cinogenicity for two major reasons. First, increased mitotic activ-
ity increases the probability that mutations may occur because 
the shortened Gl phase allows less time for DNA repair. Although 
repair still may be feasible after replication, postreplication repair 
is error-prone. Second, increased proliferation enables clonal 
expansion of the initiated cells, and this facilitated growth contrib-
utes to tumor formation and progression. The action of estrogen-
induced tumors exemplifies the interaction between DNA-reactive 
and non-DNA-reactive mechanisms. Estrogen metabolites include 
reactive quinone and hydroquinone intermediates that can produce 
mutagenic free radicals via redox cycling. Concurrently, estrogens 
induce receptor-mediated cell proliferation. Similarly, genotoxic 
carcinogens can also cause epigenetic alterations. For example, 
2-acetylaminofluorene forms DNA adducts, but also increases 
DNA methylation in numerous genes that silences a variety of 
tumor suppressor proteins (Pogribny et al., 2011). p16, ARF, 
and MGMT are also hypermethylated in aflatoxin B1–induced

Figure 3-34.   The role and regulation of the guardian of the genome: p53 tumor suppressor protein. When activated by DNA damage, p53 mediates cell cycle 
arrest, DNA repair, and apoptosis. p53 acts chiefly as a transcription factor that can activate the transcription of most target genes, while repressing some of 
others, such as those marked with (–) in the figure. For example, p53 transactivates p21 and gadd45 genes, but it represses the Cdk1 and cyclin B1 genes, p53 
also represses the genes of antiapoptotic proteins (e.g., Bcl-2 and IGF-1 receptor), to promote apoptosis. These (and other) p53-induced proapoptotic mecha-
nisms may be cell-specific, such that they are not necessarily occurring in the same cell at the same time. The intracellular level and activity of p53 depends 
primarily on the presence of mdm2 which inactivates p53 by ubiquitinating it. In fact, overexpression of mdm2 can lead to constitutive inhibition of p53 and 
thereby promotes oncogenesis even if the p53 gene is unaltered.

mouse lung tumor and in 7,12-dimethylbenzanthracene-induced 
skin tumor. Thus, epigenetic alterations evoked by genotoxic 
carcinogens may contribute to carcinogenesis after the initiating 
genotoxic event (Pogribny et al., 2011). Conversely, global DNA 
hypomethylation and tumor suppressor gene hypermethylation 
may increase the occurrence of mutations in cells exposed to non-
genotoxic carcinogens.
Failure to Execute Apoptosis Promotes Mutation and Clonal 
Growth  In many cases, initiated preneoplastic cells have much 
higher apoptotic activity than do normal cells (Fox and MacFarlane, 
2016), a mechanism designed to counteract clonal expansion. It 
follows then that inhibition of apoptosis is detrimental because 
it facilitates the fixing of mutations and allows for clonal expan-
sion of preneoplastic cells. This is the case for the pathogenesis of 
human B-cell lymphomas, in which there is chromosomal trans-
location associated with aberrantly increased expression of Bcl-2 
protein to override programmed cell death by inactivating the proa-
poptotic Bax protein. Increased levels of Bcl-2 are also detected 
in other types of cancer, and a high Bcl-2/Bax ratio in a tumor is a 
marker for poor prognosis.
RESEARCH APPROACHES TO DETERMINE 
MECHANISMS OF TOXICITY: A CASE 
STUDY
Understanding how all of the processes outlined in this chapter 
influence toxic outcome is fundamental to understanding mech-
anisms of toxicity. However, understanding how to apply these 
concepts to defining a mechanism of toxicity requires incorpo-
rating them into actual cases. Throughout this chapter, examples 
demonstrating how disposition influences toxicant exposure, how 
a toxicant interacts with and alters important cellular functions, 
how biochemical, cellular, and molecular processes dictates sensi-
tivity to toxicity and likelihood of repair and adaptation, and how 
species differences govern susceptibility to toxicity have been 
provided. As such, the examples provide specific cause–effect 
relationships, but in isolation, they are not an integrated example 
that illustrates a mechanism of toxicity as a continuum from the 
key events that initiate the toxic process to the final outcome of 
cell dysfunction or disease process. Throughout this textbook, 
there are many examples of toxic mechanisms focused on spe-
cific organ systems. However, to conclude this chapter, an exam-
ple of a comprehensive mechanism of toxicity and the research 
approaches used to define the mechanism are summarized. The 
case example is d-limonene-induced nephrotoxicity and carcino-
genicity, a classic and comprehensive example of a well-defined 
mechanism of toxicity that helped to establish how information 
on mechanisms of toxicity can inform human risk assessment 
(Lehman-McKeeman, 2018).
d-Limonene is an oil found in citrus, primarily oranges, and 
it is widely used in commerce as a flavor and fragrance. After oral 
dosing to rats, it produced evidence of renal toxicity, although only 
in males, and with chronic dosing, renal toxicity progressed to renal 
cell injury, cell proliferation, and ultimately to the development of 
renal tubular tumors. The first clue regarding toxic mechanisms was 
histopathologic evaluation of kidneys after single dosages of the 
compound. These studies showed evidence of droplets in the renal 
cortex, and specifically in proximal tubule cells, when evaluated by 
routine hematoxylin and eosin (H&E) staining. More specific stain-
ing methods for protein deposits (Mallory–Heidenhain staining) 
confirmed that these droplets were proteinaceous. Based on histo-
pathologic evidence of protein accumulation, the renal cortices from 
d-limonene-treated rats were analyzed to determine whether the 
protein accumulation was generalized to all filtered proteins or more 
specific to a few proteins. Based on two-dimensional gel electropho-
resis, only one protein was accumulating in male rat kidneys, and 
this protein was sequenced to confirm its identity as α2u-globulin.
The identification of α2u-globulin provided a foundation to 
understanding why renal toxicity was only seen in male rats because 
this protein is regulated by numerous hormones and is deemed to 
be a male rat-specific protein. Female rats have the α2u-globulin 
gene, but its expression is effectively repressed by estrogen. The 
next step in deducing the mechanism of toxicity was to determine 
how d-limonene caused α2u-globulin to accumulate in the renal 
cortex and how this protein could lead to renal toxicity. To address 
these issues, research efforts were directed toward establishing the 
fate of d-limonene and whether d-limonene or its metabolites inter-
acted with α2u-globulin.
The fate of d-limonene had been characterized based on 
metabolism profiles obtained from urine or in hepatocyte cultures. 
These metabolism studies suggested that there were no likely 
reactive metabolites or metabolites that would be expected to be 
directly toxic. The critical question was whether d-limonene or its 
metabolites were detected in the kidney, and if so, whether any of 
them bound to α2u-globulin. Such studies were facilitated by the 
use of radiolabeled d-limonene, and equilibrium dialysis experi-
ments showed that radioactivity was detected in a high-molecular-
weight fraction (protein) of male rat kidney homogenates. Based 
on evidence of protein binding, additional studies, gas-chromatog-
raphy–mass spectrometry (GC-MS) analyses were used to identify 
a d-limonene metabolite in the protein fraction, and the metabolite 
was one that had not previously been reported. Specifically, d-lim-
onene-1,2-epoxide was identified in the dialyzed protein fraction of 
male rat kidney homogenates, but this metabolite was not detected in 
kidney homogenates from d-limonene-treated female rats. Although 
it is generally considered that epoxides are unstable metabolites that 
either react covalently with macromolecules or are hydrolyzed to a 
corresponding diol metabolite by epoxide hydrolase (described pre-
viously; see Chap. 6), d-limonene-1,2-epoxide was an exception to 
this general rule because it is very stable, bound reversibly to renal 
proteins, and did not hydrolyze to its corresponding diol.
To address questions regarding how d-limonene-1,2-epoxide 
might cause α2u-globulin to accumulate, the protein was purified, 
and in vitro studies confirmed that the epoxide bound reversibly to 
α2u-globulin with a dissociation constant of approximately 10−7 M. 
Additional studies established that binding of d-limonene-1, 
2-epoxide to α2u-globulin reduced the lysosomal degradation of the 
protein relative to the native protein. Specifically, the rate of degra-
dation by renal cortical lysosomal lysates decreased by about 30%, 
and this change was specific to α2u-globulin because the lysosomal 
degradation of other proteins such as albumin was not altered by the 
presence of the epoxide and there were no changes in lysosomal 
cathepsin activity in d-limonene-treated rat kidneys.
Although the ligand–protein complex can be isolated from the 
kidney in vivo, one part of the mechanism that is not defined is 
where the interaction between d-limonene-1,2-epoxide and α2u-
globulin occurs. However, given that the complex appears to be 
localized in the renal phagolysosomal compartment, it is likely that 
the complex does not form in the kidney. Rather, the interaction 
probably occurs outside the kidney (in liver or blood), and follow-
ing glomerular filtration, the complex enters the proximal tubule 
cells by endocytosis and accumulates in the phagolysosomes after 
lysosomal fusion.
Collectively, the data summarized thus far indicate that 
formation of d-limonene-1,2-epoxide and its ability to bind to

Mechanisms of Toxicity
Chapter 3
121
α2u-globulin are key events that lead to the accumulation of pro-
tein and initiate d-limonene-induced renal toxicity (Fig. 3-35). 
However, as the duration of treatment continues, the lysosomes 
become enlarged, engorged with protein and polyangular in shape 
with ensuing cytotoxicity presenting as single cell necrosis in the 
P2 segment of the proximal tubule. Thus, necrosis is likely to be 
the mechanism of cell death, but it is unclear as to how an engorged 
lysosomal compartment is cytotoxic. Commensurate with cell 
necrosis, there is a dose- and time-dependent loss in renal func-
tion. Renal functional changes observed include reduced uptake of 
organic anions, cations, and amino acids, a mild proteinuria and 
increased levels of renal toxicity biomarkers, particularly gluta-
thione S-transferases (α and µ isoforms), have been reported. In 
response to cell death and functional changes, there is cell prolif-
eration in the kidney, most notably in the P2 segment of the proxi-
mal tubules, the site of the protein accumulation. With continued 
treatment, the cell proliferation continues, but does not fully restore 
renal function. The sustained increase in renal cell proliferation is 
believed to exert a promotional influence on the kidney, such that 
sustained cell turnover is mechanistically linked to the development 
of renal tubular tumors. Thus, α2u-globulin nephropathy begins as 
protein accumulation, but represents a continuum of changes that 
ultimately progress to renal tumors. The major events in this con-
tinuum are summarized in Fig. 3-35, with application and illustra-
tion of the four-component framework outlined for mechanisms of 
toxicity developed in this chapter (Lehman-McKeeman, 2018).
CONCLUSIONS
Mechanisms of Toxicity and Adverse 
Outcome Pathways
A “real world” application of mechanisms of toxicity is the devel-
opment of adverse outcome pathways (AOPs) to inform and facili-
tate toxicity testing and hazard identification. Basically, an AOP 
is a systematic approach that organizes and integrates a series of 
biological events such as those described throughout this chapter 
that are likely to lead to an adverse outcome. AOPs place emphasis 
on the key events, often called molecular initiating events that link 
a causal chain of endpoints to the final toxicity. These events are 
also organized into biological levels ranging from macromolecu-
lar effects to those observed at the cellular, organ, and organism 
level (Burden et al., 2015; Wittwehr et al., 2017). Overall, an AOP 
is a general pathway that is somewhat similar to that outlined at 
the beginning of this chapter (Fig. 3-1), and the molecular initi-
ating events include many of the fundamental mechanistic effects 
outlined in this chapter. However, the AOP approach provides for 
a systematic integration of existing knowledge on chemical mode 
of action, starting with a molecular initiating event and continu-
ing through numerous key events that culminate in a toxic effect 
(Fig. 3-36). Additionally, integration of mechanisms of toxicity 
into AOPs may be beneficial for prioritizing compounds for more 
defined toxicity testing in animals or as a stand-alone system for 
predicting toxicity. Currently, the proposed applications and utility 
of AOPs focus on their potential to (1) advance the use of in vitro, 
nonanimal methods in safety evaluation; (2) extrapolate across 
species, with emphasis on increasing confidence of human safety; 
and (3) identify potential mechanism-based biomarkers of toxicity. 
While these approaches may bring more integrated systems biol-
ogy approaches to assessing mechanisms of toxicity, the tools must 
be able to broadly integrate all aspects of toxic mechanisms pre-
sented in this chapter. That is, a useful tool will have to encompass 
the broad aspects of chemical disposition and biotransformation, 
and inform quantitative relationships describing when cellular pro-
tective mechanisms are overwhelmed thereby initiating or propa-
gating the chain of key events that lead to toxicity. The application 
of AOPs in mechanistic and predictive toxicology is an emerging 
area, but it is not yet clear whether, and if so how, these approaches 
will advance the application of mechanism of toxicity into hazard 
identification and risk assessment.

Figure 3-35.   The mode of action for d-limonene-induced hyaline droplet nephropathy and renal carcinogenesis. The key events in the mechanism of action by 
which d-limonene, a naturally occurring terpene (oil of orange), causes kidney cancer in male rats. The mechanism requires biotransformation to d-limonene-
1,2-epoxide which binds reversibly to α2u-globulin in male rats, and the interaction with the protein reduces its normal degradation in renal lysosomes leading 
to marked accumulation of the protein and histological evidence of hyaline droplets. With chronic exposure, there is sustained injury in the proximal tubules, 
with compensatory cell proliferation, which ultimately increases the risk of renal cancer. This syndrome is unique to male rats because only male rats synthesize 
α2u-globulin, and the renal syndrome described only occurs with this male rat-specific protein. In summarizing this mechanism, the components of the critical 
events involved in mechanisms of toxicity (Fig. 3-1) are also shown to illustrate how every step discussed in the chapter can be represented by the example of 
hyaline droplet nephropathy (also called α2u-globulin nephropathy).

General Principles of Toxicology
Unit I
The Importance of Mechanisms of Toxicity
In this chapter, a simplified scheme has been used as an overview 
of the development of toxicity (Fig. 3-1 A and B), and the poten-
tial events that follow toxicant exposure and contribute to toxicity 
have been presented. In completing the chapter, it should be read-
ily apparent that the route to toxicity can be considerably diverse 
and complicated, as described by the many examples throughout 
the chapter and by the comprehensive case study of d-limonene. 
This chapter is a compendium of the fundamental processes 
that determine toxicity and provides the foundation for more in-
depth understanding of specific toxicities that are discussed in 
detail throughout other chapters. The basic concepts of any toxic 
mechanism are essential to identifying key events associated with 
toxic outcome and inform species differences in toxicity, with 
particular emphasis on informing the human relevance of tox-
icity. Moreover, understanding mechanisms of toxicity provides 
opportunities and direction to identifying biomarkers of toxicity, 
tools to predict toxicity, and potential ways to mitigate adverse 
effects.

Figure 3-36.   Overview of adverse outcome pathways. Adverse outcome pathways (AOPs) are a systematic organization of events that can lead to a toxic effect. 
Key events are those that are causally related to the final toxicity, and as illustrated can be organized into those observed at the molecular, cellular, and organ 
levels. Overall, an AOP is very similar to that outlined in Fig. 3-1, as highlighted in the figure.
